If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  is statistically sound with soundness error  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$ ,  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  is statistically binding in binding mode, and  $2^{\ell} \cdot \varepsilon = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  then  $\Pi_{\mathsf{dvNIZK}}$  from Construction 3.4 satisfies adaptive statistical soundness.

The proof of Theorem 3.7 is very similar to the corresponding proof of adaptive statistical soundness from [QRW19]. We include it in Appendix A.

<span id="page-19-1"></span>**Theorem 3.8** (Statistical Zero-Knowledge in Hiding Mode). If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies statistical (resp., perfect) zero-knowledge and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  provides statistical (resp., perfect) simulation in hiding mode, then  $\Pi_{\mathsf{dvNIZK}}$  from Construction 3.4 satisfies statistical (resp., perfect) zero-knowledge in hiding mode.

*Proof.* Let  $\mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  denote the zero-knowledge simulator for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  and  $\mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG}} = (\mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG},1}, \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG},2})$  be the simulator for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  in hiding mode. We construct a simulator  $\mathcal{S} = (\mathcal{S}_1, \mathcal{S}_2)$  as follows:

- $S_1(1^{\lambda}) \to (\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{sk}})$ : Run  $(\operatorname{st}_{\operatorname{HBG}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}_{\operatorname{HBG}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}_{\operatorname{HBG}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{sk}}_{\operatorname{HBG}}) \leftarrow S_{\operatorname{HBG},1}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$ . Choose  $\widetilde{s} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}^{\rho}$  and set  $\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = \operatorname{st}_{\operatorname{HBG}}$ ,  $\widetilde{\operatorname{crs}} = (\lambda, s, \widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}_{\operatorname{HBG}})$ ,  $\widetilde{\operatorname{pk}} = \widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}_{\operatorname{HBG}}$ , and  $\widetilde{\operatorname{sk}} = \widetilde{\operatorname{sk}}_{\operatorname{HBG}}$ . Output  $(\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{sk}})$ .
- $S_2(\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, x) \to \widetilde{\pi}$ : On input  $\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = \operatorname{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  and  $x \in \{0, 1\}^n$ , run  $(\widetilde{I}, \widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}}, \widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \leftarrow S_{\mathsf{HBM}}(1^{\lambda}, x)$  and  $(\widetilde{\sigma}, \{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i \in \widetilde{I}}) \leftarrow S_{\mathsf{HBG},2}(\operatorname{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \widetilde{I}, \widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}} \oplus \widetilde{s}_{\widetilde{I}})$ . Output the simulated proof  $\widetilde{\pi} = (\widetilde{\sigma}, \widetilde{I}, \widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}} \oplus \widetilde{s}_{\widetilde{I}}, \{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i \in \widetilde{I}}, \widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}})$ .

To complete the proof, we proceed via a hybrid argument:

- $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ : This is the real distribution. Namely, the challenger begins by sampling  $\mathsf{crs} \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$  and  $(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{crs})$ , and gives  $(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk})$  to the adversary. The challenger responds to oracle queries on inputs (x,w) with  $\pi \leftarrow \mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{pk},x,w)$  if  $\mathcal{R}(x,w)=1$  and with  $\bot$  otherwise.
  - In more detail, the challenger samples  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\rho}$  and runs  $\operatorname{crs_{HBG}} \leftarrow \operatorname{HBG.Setup}(1^{\lambda},1^{\rho},\operatorname{hiding})$ , and  $(\operatorname{pk_{HBG}},\operatorname{sk_{HBG}}) \leftarrow \operatorname{HBG.KeyGen}(\operatorname{crs_{HBG}})$ . It sets  $\operatorname{crs} = (\lambda,s,\operatorname{crs_{HBG}})$ ,  $\operatorname{pk} = \operatorname{pk_{HBG}}$ , and  $\operatorname{sk} = \operatorname{sk_{HBG}}$ . When the adversary makes an oracle query on (x,w) where  $\mathcal{R}(x,w) = 1$ , the challenger computes  $(\sigma,r,\{\pi_{\operatorname{HBG},i}\}_{i\in[\rho]}) \leftarrow \operatorname{HBG.GenBits}(\operatorname{crs_{HBG}},\operatorname{pk_{HBG}})$  and  $(I,\pi_{\operatorname{HBM}}) \leftarrow \operatorname{HBM.Prove}(1^{\lambda},r\oplus s,x,w)$ . It outputs  $\pi = (\sigma,I,r_I,\{\pi_{\operatorname{HBG},i}\}_{i\in I},\pi_{\operatorname{HBM}})$ .
- $Hyb_1$ : Same as  $Hyb_0$ , except the challenger uses  $S_{HBG,1}$  to generate the common reference string and public/secret keys. It uses  $S_{HBG,2}$  to simulate the openings to the hidden-bits generator when responding to oracle queries. Specifically, the challenger works as follows:
  - 1. At the start of the game, the challenger runs  $(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG},1}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$ . It samples  $\widetilde{s} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}^{\rho}$  and gives  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\lambda, \widetilde{s}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}), \ \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}} = \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \ \mathrm{and} \ \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}} = \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}}_{\mathsf{HBG}} \ \mathrm{to} \ \mathrm{the} \ \mathrm{adversary}$ .
  - 2. Whenever the adversary makes an oracle query (x, w) where  $\mathcal{R}(x, w) = 0$ , the challenger replies with  $\bot$ . Otherwise, it samples  $r \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}^{\rho}$  and runs  $(I, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{HBM.Prove}(1^{\lambda}, r \oplus \widetilde{s}, x, w)$ . Then, it samples  $(\widetilde{\sigma}, \{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i \in I}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG},2}(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, I, r_I)$ , and finally outputs the proof  $\pi = (\widetilde{\sigma}, I, r_I, \{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i \in I}, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}})$ .
- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub>, except when responding to oracle queries, the challenger uses the simulator for the hidden-bits model NIZK to simulate the proofs. More precisely, on input (x,w) where  $\mathcal{R}(x,w)=1$ , the challenger computes  $(\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}'_{\widetilde{I}},\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}})\leftarrow\mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBM}}(1^{\lambda},x)$ . It sets  $\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}}=\widetilde{r}'_{\widetilde{I}}\oplus\widetilde{s}_{\widetilde{I}}$ , and computes  $(\widetilde{\sigma},\{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i\in I})\leftarrow\mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG},2}(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}},\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}})$  and outputs the proof  $\widetilde{\pi}=(\widetilde{\sigma},\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}},\{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i\in \widetilde{I}},\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}})$ . This is the simulated distribution (up to interchanging the labels  $\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}}$  and  $\widetilde{r}'_{\widetilde{I}}$ ).

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output distribution of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that the output distributions of each adjacent pair of hybrid experiments are statistically indistinguishable.

<span id="page-20-0"></span>**Lemma 3.9.** If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  satisfies statistical (resp., perfect) simulation in hiding mode, then the output distributions of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  are statistically (resp., perfectly) indistinguishable.

*Proof.* Suppose there exists an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  such that  $|\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) = 1] - \Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) = 1]| = \varepsilon$  for some non-negligible  $\varepsilon$ . We use  $\mathcal{A}$  to construct an adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{B}, \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, 0]$  and  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{B}, \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, 1]$  are distinguishable (with the same advantage  $\varepsilon$ ). Algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  works as follows:

- First, algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  receives a tuple  $(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{HBG}})$  from the challenger. It samples  $s \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\rho}$ , and gives  $\mathsf{crs} = (\lambda, s, \mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}})$ ,  $\mathsf{pk} = \mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{HBG}}$ , and  $\mathsf{sk} = \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- When  $\mathcal{A}$  makes a query on (x, w), if  $\mathcal{R}(x, w) = 0$ , algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  responds with  $\bot$ . Otherwise,  $\mathcal{B}$  makes a challenge query to its challenger to obtain a string  $r \in \{0, 1\}^{\rho}$ . Algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  computes  $(I, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{HBM}.\mathsf{Prove}(1^{\lambda}, r \oplus s, x, w)$  and gives  $I \subseteq [\rho]$  to the challenger.

The challenger replies with a pair  $(\sigma, \{\pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i\in I})$ . Finally,  $\mathcal{B}$  replies to  $\mathcal{A}$  with the proof  $\pi = (\sigma, I, r_I, \{\pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i\in I}, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}})$ .

 $\bullet$  At the end of the game,  ${\mathcal B}$  outputs whatever  ${\mathcal A}$  outputs.

By construction, if the challenger generates the parameters and answers the queries according to  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{B}, \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, 0]$ , then  $\mathcal{B}$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  for  $\mathcal{A}$ . Alternatively, if the challenger implements the logic according to  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{B}, \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, 1]$ , then  $\mathcal{B}$  perfectly simulates  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  for  $\mathcal{A}$ .  $\square$ 

<span id="page-21-2"></span>**Lemma 3.10.** If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies statistical (resp., perfect) zero-knowledge, then the output distributions of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  are statistically (resp., perfectly) indistinguishable.

Proof. We define a sequence of q+1 intermediate hybrid experiments  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,0},\ldots,\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,q}$ , where  $q=\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  is a bound on the number of queries the adversary makes. Hybrid  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$  is the experiment where the first i oracle queries are handled according to the procedure in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  and the remaining queries are handled according to the procedure in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . By construction  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,0} \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,q} \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_2$ . Moreover, each adjacent pair of hybrid experiments  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  only differ in how the  $i^{th}$  oracle query (x,w) is handled. In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$ , the challenger samples a random  $r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\rho}$  and computes  $(I,\pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{HBM}.\mathsf{Prove}(1^{\lambda},r \oplus \widetilde{s},x,w)$  while in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$ , the challenger invokes the simulator  $(\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}}',\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_{\mathsf{HBM}}(1^{\lambda},x)$ . By statistical zero-knowledge of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$ , we have that  $(I,r_I \oplus \widetilde{s}_I,\pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \stackrel{s}{\approx} (\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}}',\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}})$ . If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies perfect zero-knowledge, then these two distributions are identically distributed. Correspondingly, hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,i}$  are statistically indistinguishable (or identically distributed if  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies perfect zero-knowledge). Since  $q=\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , we conclude by a hybrid argument that the outputs of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  are statistically indistinguishable (or identically distributed if  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies perfect zero-knowledge).

Since each consecutive pair of hybrid experiments is statistically indistinguishable (or identically distributed), the claim follows.  $\Box$ 

<span id="page-21-1"></span>Theorem 3.11 (Statistical Zero-Knowledge against Malicious Verifiers). If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies statistical zero-knowledge and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  provides statistical simulation for malicious keys, then Construction 3.4 is a MDV-NIZK. Namely, Construction 3.4 satisfies statistical zero-knowledge against malicious verifiers in hiding mode.

The proof of Theorem 3.11 follows from a similar argument as Theorem 3.8 and is included in Appendix A.

# <span id="page-21-0"></span>4 Dual-Mode HBGs from the k-Lin Assumption

In this section, we show how to construct dual-mode hidden-bits generators from the k-Lin assumption. We begin with a basic construction from the k-Lin assumption (Section 4.1) and then show how to extend it to achieve public verifiability in a pairing group (Section 4.2) as well as how to achieve security against malicious verifiers in a pairing-free group (Section 4.3).

#### <span id="page-22-0"></span>4.1 Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generator from k-Lin

In this section, we show how to construct a dual-mode hidden-bits generator from the k-linear (k-Lin) assumption [BBS04, HK07, Sha07, EHK<sup>+</sup>13] over pairing-free groups for any  $k \ge 1$ . We note that the 1-Lin assumption is precisely the decisional Diffie-Hellman (DDH) assumption. We begin by recalling some basic notation as well as the k-Lin assumption.

**Notation.** Throughout this section, we will work with cyclic groups  $\mathbb{G}$  of prime order p. We will use multiplicative notation to denote the group operation. For  $x \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , we often refer to  $g^x$  as an "encoding" of x. For a matrix  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n \times m}$ , we write  $g^{\mathbf{A}} \in \mathbb{G}^{n \times m}$  to denote the matrix of group elements formed by taking the element-wise encoding of each component of  $\mathbf{A}$ .

**Definition 4.1** (Prime-Order Group Generator). A prime-order group generator algorithm Group Generator) an efficient algorithm that on input the security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  outputs a description  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g)$  of a prime-order group  $\mathbb{G}$  with order p and generator g. Throughout this work, we will assume that  $1/p = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

**Definition 4.2** (k-Linear Assumption [BBS04, HK07, Sha07, EHK<sup>+</sup>13]). Fix a constant  $k \geq 1$ . We say that a prime-order group generator GroupGen satisfies the k-Lin assumption if for all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  and sampling  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow \mathsf{GroupGen}(1^{\lambda})$ ,  $\mathbf{a} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ ,  $\mathbf{w} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}$ , and  $\mathbf{u} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , we have that

$$\left|\Pr[\mathcal{A}(\mathcal{G},g^{\mathbf{A}},g^{\mathbf{A}\mathbf{w}})=1]-\Pr[\mathcal{A}(\mathcal{G},g^{\mathbf{A}},g^{\mathbf{u}})=1]\right|=\mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

<span id="page-22-2"></span>where

$$\mathbf{A} = \left(\frac{\operatorname{diag}(\mathbf{a})}{\mathbf{1}^{\mathsf{T}}}\right) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1)\times k},\tag{4.1}$$

and diag( $\mathbf{a}$ )  $\in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times k}$  denotes the diagonal matrix whose entries are  $a_1, \dots, a_k$ , and  $\mathbf{1} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}$  is the all-ones vector.

<span id="page-22-1"></span>Construction 4.3 (Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generator from k-Lin). Let GroupGen be a prime-order group generator algorithm. We construct a dual-mode hidden-bits generator (HBG) as follows:

- Setup $(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \text{mode}) \to \text{crs}$ : First, the setup algorithm samples  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow \text{GroupGen}(1^{\lambda})$  and a hash function  $H \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$ , where  $\mathcal{H}$  is a family of hash functions with domain  $\mathbb{G}$  and range  $\{0, 1\}$ . Next, it samples  $\mathbf{V} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times k}$  and vectors  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\rho} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$  as follows:
  - If mode = hiding, sample  $\mathbf{w}_i \stackrel{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$  for all  $i \in [\rho]$ .
  - If mode = binding, sample  $\mathbf{s}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_n^k$  and set  $\mathbf{w}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$  for all  $i \in [\rho]$ .

Output  $\operatorname{crs} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\rho}}).$ 

• KeyGen(crs)  $\to$  (pk, sk): On input crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\rho}})$ , the key-generation algorithm samples  $a \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and  $\mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\rho} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ . For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , it sets  $\mathbf{z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ . It outputs

$$\mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_\rho}) \quad \text{and} \quad \mathsf{sk} = (a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_\rho).$$

• GenBits(crs, pk) → (σ, r, {πi}i∈[ρ] ): On input crs = (G, H, gV, gw<sup>1</sup> , . . . , gw<sup>ρ</sup> ) and public key pk = (g z1 , . . . , gz<sup>ρ</sup> ), sample y <sup>r</sup><sup>←</sup> <sup>Z</sup> ρ+k <sup>p</sup> and compute for each i ∈ [ρ],

$$g^{t_i} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{w}_i}$$
 and  $g^{u_i} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}_i}$ .

Next, let σ = g y <sup>T</sup>V. For each i ∈ [ρ], set r<sup>i</sup> ← H(g <sup>t</sup><sup>i</sup> ) and π<sup>i</sup> ← (g ti , gu<sup>i</sup> ), and output σ, r, and {πi}i∈[ρ] .

• Verify(crs,sk, σ, i, r<sup>i</sup> , πi): On input crs = (G, H, gV, gw<sup>1</sup> , . . . , gw<sup>ρ</sup> ), secret key sk = (a, b1, . . . , bρ), σ = g c T , i ∈ [ρ], r<sup>i</sup> ∈ {0, 1}, and π<sup>i</sup> = (g ti , gu<sup>i</sup> ), output 1 if g <sup>u</sup><sup>i</sup> = (g tia )(g c <sup>T</sup>b<sup>i</sup> ) and r<sup>i</sup> = H(g <sup>t</sup><sup>i</sup> ). Otherwise, output 0.

Correctness and security analysis. We now state the correctness and security theorems for Construction [4.3](#page-22-1) and give the proofs in Appendix [C.1.](#page-39-0)

<span id="page-23-2"></span>Theorem 4.4 (Correctness). Construction [4.3](#page-22-1) is correct.

<span id="page-23-4"></span>Theorem 4.5 (Succinctness). Construction [4.3](#page-22-1) is succinct.

<span id="page-23-5"></span>Theorem 4.6 (CRS Indistinguishability). Suppose the k-Lin assumption holds for GroupGen. Then, Construction [3.4](#page-18-1) satisfies CRS indistinguishability.

<span id="page-23-6"></span>Theorem 4.7 (Statistical Binding in Binding Mode). Construction [4.3](#page-22-1) satisfies statistical binding in binding mode.

<span id="page-23-3"></span>Theorem 4.8 (Statistical Simulation in Hiding Mode). If H satisfies statistical uniformity, then Construction [4.3](#page-22-1) satisfies statistical simulation in hiding mode.

Remark 4.9 (Common Random String in Hiding Mode). Construction [4.3](#page-22-1) has the property that in hiding mode, the CRS is a collection of uniformly random group elements; in other words, the CRS in hiding mode can be sampled as a common random string. In conjunction with Construction [3.4,](#page-18-1) we obtain a statistical NIZK argument in the common random string model (and a computational NIZK proof in the common reference string model).

### <span id="page-23-0"></span>4.2 Publicly-Verifiable Hidden-Bit Generators from Pairings

In this section, we describe a variant of our dual-mode hidden-bits generator from Section [4.1](#page-22-0) to obtain a publicly-verifiable hidden-bits generator from pairings. Our resulting construction does not give a dual-mode hidden-bits generator. Instead, we obtain a standard HBG (where there is a single mode) that satisfies statistical simulation and computational binding. Using an analog of Construction [3.4,](#page-18-1) this suffices to construct a publicly-verifiable statistical NIZK argument. We refer to Appendix [B](#page-38-0) for the details. Below, we define the computational binding property we use:

<span id="page-23-1"></span>Definition 4.10 (Computational Binding). A publicly-verifiable hidden bits generator ΠHBG = (Setup, GenBits, Verify) is computationally binding if the following property holds:

• Computational binding: There exists an efficient extractor E = (E1, E2), where E<sup>2</sup> is deterministic, and for all polynomials ρ = ρ(λ), the following two properties hold:

CRS indistinguishability: The following distributions are computationally indistinguishable:

$$\{\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda},1^{\rho})\} \overset{c}{\approx} \{(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}},\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{E}_1(1^{\lambda},1^{\rho}) : \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}\}.$$

- **Binding:** For all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , and sampling  $(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{E}_1(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$  followed by  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}})$  and  $r \leftarrow \mathcal{E}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \sigma^*)$ , we have that

$$\Pr[r_{i^*} \neq r^* \land \mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

**Pairing groups.** In this section, we work in (asymmetric) pairing groups. We review the notion of a pairing below, as well as the kernel k-linear (k-KerLin) assumption [MRV15, KW15], which can be viewed as a search version of the k-linear assumption. As shown in [KW15], the k-KerLin assumption is weaker than the k-Lin assumption (in particular, k-Lin implies k-KerLin), but stronger than the CDH assumption.

**Definition 4.11** (Prime-Order Pairing-Group Generator). A prime-order (asymmetric) pairing group generator algorithm PairingGroupGen is an efficient algorithm that on input the security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  outputs a description  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, g_1, g_2, e)$  of two base groups  $\mathbb{G}_1$  (generated by  $g_1$ ),  $\mathbb{G}_2$  (generated by  $g_2$ ), and a target group  $\mathbb{G}_T$ , all of prime order p, together with an efficiently-computable mapping  $e: \mathbb{G}_1 \times \mathbb{G}_2 \to \mathbb{G}_T$  (called the "pairing"). Finally, the mapping e is bilinear: for all  $x, y \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $e(g_1^x, g_2^y) = e(g_1, g_2)^{xy}$ .

**Definition 4.12** (Kernel *k*-Linear Assumption [MRV15, KW15]). The kernel *k*-linear (*k*-KerLin) assumption holds in  $\mathbb{G}_2$  relative to a pairing-group generator PairingGroupGen if for all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , and sampling  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, g_1, g_2, e) \leftarrow \mathsf{PairingGroupGen}(1^{\lambda})$ ,  $\mathbf{a} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and defining  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times k}$  as in Eq. (4.1), the following holds:

$$\Pr[g_1^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}} \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathcal{G}, g_2^{\mathbf{A}}) : \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A} = \mathbf{0} \land \mathbf{c} \neq \mathbf{0}] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

We can define an analogous assumption over  $\mathbb{G}_1$  (by interchanging the roles of  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and  $\mathbb{G}_2$  above).

**Notation.** For a matrix **A**, we continue to write  $g_1^{\mathbf{A}}$  and  $g_2^{\mathbf{A}}$  to denote matrices of group elements (over  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and  $\mathbb{G}_2$ , respectively). In addition, if we have two matrices  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times \ell}$  and  $\mathbf{B} \in \mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times n}$ , we write  $e(g_1^{\mathbf{A}}, g_2^{\mathbf{B}})$  to denote the operation that outputs  $e(g_1, g_2)^{\mathbf{A}\mathbf{B}} \in \mathbb{G}_T^{m \times n}$ . In particular, the  $(i, j)^{\text{th}}$  entry of  $e(g_1^{\mathbf{A}}, g_2^{\mathbf{B}})$  is computed as

$$\left[e(g_1^{\mathbf{A}},g_2^{\mathbf{B}})\right]_{i,j} = \prod_{k \in [\ell]} e(g_1^{a_{i,k}},g_2^{b_{k,j}}).$$

<span id="page-24-0"></span>Construction 4.13 (Publicly-Verifiable Hidden-Bits Generator from Pairings). Let PairingGroupGen be a prime-order bilinear group generator algorithm. We construct a publicly-verifiable hidden-bits generator (HBG) as follow:

• Setup $(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}) \to \text{crs}$ : The setup algorithm starts by sampling  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, g_1, g_2, e) \leftarrow \text{PairingGroupGen}(1^{\lambda})$  and a hash function  $H \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$  where  $\mathcal{H}$  is a family of hash functions with domain  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and range  $\{0, 1\}$ . Next, it samples a matrix  $\mathbf{V} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times k}$ , vectors  $\mathbf{w}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{w}_k \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times k}$ 

 $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ , and verification components  $\mathbf{a} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}$ ,  $\mathbf{B}_1, \dots, \mathbf{B}_{\rho} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times (k+1)}$ . In addition, it samples  $\mathbf{d} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and constructs the matrix

<span id="page-25-7"></span>
$$\mathbf{D} = \left(\frac{\operatorname{diag}(\mathbf{d})}{\mathbf{1}^{\mathsf{T}}}\right) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1)\times k}.$$
 (4.2)

It computes  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}^{\mathsf{T}} \leftarrow \mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{D} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , it computes  $\mathbf{Z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{w}_i \mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}} + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{B}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times(k+1)}$  and  $\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{B}_i \mathbf{D} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k\times k}$ . It outputs

$$\mathsf{crs} = \left(\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^\mathbf{V}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^\mathbf{D}, \left\{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}\right\}_{i \in [\rho]}\right).$$

• GenBits(crs)  $\rightarrow (\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [k]})$ : On input crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^{\mathbf{V}}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^{\mathsf{T}}}, g_2^{\mathbf{D}}, \{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}\}_{i \in [\rho]})$ , sample  $\mathbf{y} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ , and compute for each  $i \in [\rho]$ ,

$$g_1^{t_i} \leftarrow g_1^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{w}_i} \quad \text{and} \quad g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^\mathsf{T}} \leftarrow g_1^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{Z}_i}.$$

Next, let  $\sigma = g_1^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{V}}$ , and for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , set  $r_i \leftarrow H(g_1^{t_i})$  and  $\pi_i = (g_1^{t_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^\mathsf{T}})$ . Output  $\sigma$ , r, and  $\{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}$ .

• Verify(crs,  $\sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i$ ): On input crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^{\mathbf{V}}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\mathbf{D}}, \{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}\}_{i \in [\rho]}), \ \sigma = g_1^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}, \ i \in [\rho], r_i \in \{0, 1\}, \ \text{and} \ \pi_i = (g_1^{t_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^\mathsf{T}}), \ \text{output} \ 1 \ \text{if}$ 

<span id="page-25-4"></span>
$$e(g_1^{t_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T}}) \cdot e(g_1^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}) = e(g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\mathbf{D}})$$
 (4.3)

and  $r_i = H(g_1^{t_i})$ . If either check fails, output 0.

Correctness and security analysis. We now state the correctness and security theorems for Construction 4.13 and provide the proofs in Appendix C.2.

<span id="page-25-2"></span>Theorem 4.14 (Correctness). Construction 4.13 is correct.

<span id="page-25-5"></span>Theorem 4.15 (Succinctness). Construction 4.13 is succinct.

<span id="page-25-6"></span>**Theorem 4.16** (Computational Binding). Suppose PairingGroupGen outputs groups ( $\mathbb{G}_1$ ,  $\mathbb{G}_2$ ,  $\mathbb{G}_T$ ) such that the k-Lin assumption holds in  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and the k-KerLin assumption holds in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ . Then, Construction 4.13 satisfies computational binding in binding mode.

<span id="page-25-3"></span>**Theorem 4.17** (Statistical Simulation). If  $\mathcal{H}$  satisfies statistical uniformity, then Construction 4.13 satisfies statistical simulation.

#### <span id="page-25-0"></span>4.3 Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generator with Malicious Security from k-Lin

<span id="page-25-1"></span>We now show how to modify the k-Lin construction from Section 4.1 (Construction 4.3) to obtain a hidden-bits generator with security against malicious verifiers. Combined with Construction 3.4, this yields a dual-mode MDV-NIZK (Theorem 3.11). We refer to Section 1.2 for a high-level description of our approach.

Construction 4.18 (Dual-Mode HBG with Malicious Security from k-Lin). Let  $\rho$  be the output length of the hidden-bits generator. We require the following primitives:

- Let GroupGen be a prime-order group generator algorithm.
- Let  $\ell = 3\rho\lambda$  and define  $\mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell} := \{S \subseteq [\ell] : |S| = \lambda\}$  to be the set of all subsets of  $[\ell]$  that contains exactly  $\lambda$  elements. Let  $G : \{0,1\}^{\kappa} \to \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}^{\rho} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho\ell}$  be a PRG with seed length  $\kappa = \kappa(\lambda)$ . Here, p is the order of the group  $\mathbb{G}$  output by GroupGen (on input  $1^{\lambda}$ ).

Constructing the PRG G. It is straightforward to construct a PRG with outputs in  $\mathcal{T}^{\rho}_{\lambda,\ell} \times \mathbb{Z}^{\rho\ell}_p$  from a PRG with outputs in  $\{0,1\}^{\rho\lambda\ell(1+\lceil\log p\rceil)}$ . To see this, it suffices to give an efficient algorithm that maps from the uniform distribution on  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda\ell(1+\lceil\log p\rceil)}$  to a distribution that is statistically close to uniform over  $\mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell} \times \mathbb{Z}^{\ell}_p$ . Take a string  $\gamma \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda\ell(1+\lceil\log p\rceil)}$ .

- The first  $\lambda \ell$  bits of  $\gamma$  are interpreted as  $\ell$  blocks of  $\lambda$ -bit indices  $i_1, \ldots, i_\ell \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ . These indices specify the set  $S \subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}$  as follows. First, take  $S_0 \leftarrow [\ell]$ . For each  $j \in [\lambda]$ , take  $s_j$  to be the  $(i_j \mod |S_{j-1}|)^{\text{th}}$  element of  $S_{j-1}$  and define  $S_j \leftarrow S_{j-1} \setminus \{s_j\}$ . Define  $S \leftarrow \{s_1, \ldots, s_\ell\} \in \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}$ .
- The remaining  $\lambda \ell \lceil \log p \rceil$  bits of  $\gamma$  are taken to be the binary representation of an  $\ell$ -dimensional vector  $\boldsymbol{\alpha} \in \mathbb{Z}^{\ell}$ , where each component is a  $\lambda \lceil \log p \rceil$ -bit integer.

The string  $\gamma \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda\ell(1+\lceil \log p \rceil)}$  is mapped onto  $(S, \boldsymbol{\alpha} \bmod p) \in \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell}$ . By construction, this procedure maps from the uniform distribution over  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda\ell(1+\lceil \log p \rceil)}$  to a distribution that is statistically uniform over  $\mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell}$ .

We construct the dual-mode designated-verifier hidden-bits generator with malicious security as follows:

- Setup( $1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}$ , mode)  $\rightarrow$  crs: Let  $\ell' = \rho \ell$ . Sample  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow$  GroupGen( $1^{\lambda}$ ) and  $H \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$ , where  $\mathcal{H}$  is a family of hash functions with domain  $\mathbb{G}$  and range  $\{0, 1\}$ . Next, it samples  $\mathbf{V} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k)\times k}$  and vectors  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\ell'} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'+k}$  as follows:
  - If mode = hiding, sample  $\mathbf{w}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'+k}$  for all  $i \in [\ell']$ .
  - If mode = binding, sample  $\mathbf{s}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and set  $\mathbf{w}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$  for all  $i \in [\ell']$ .

Output  $crs = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}}).$ 

• KeyGen(crs)  $\to$  (pk, sk): On input crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$ , sample  $a \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and  $\mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\ell'} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ . For each  $i \in [\ell']$ , compute  $\mathbf{z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'+k}$  and output

$$\mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}})$$
 and  $\mathsf{sk} = (a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\ell'}).$ 

• GenBits(crs, pk)  $\to$   $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]})$ : On input crs  $= (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$  and the public key  $\mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}})$ , sample  $\mathbf{y} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell' + k}$  and compute for each  $i \in [\ell']$ 

$$g^{t_i} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{w}_i$$
 and  $g^{u_i} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{z}_i$ .

Next, sample a PRG seed  $\mathbf{s} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\kappa}$  and compute  $(\hat{S}_1,\ldots,\hat{S}_{\rho},\boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(\mathbf{s})$  where  $\hat{S}_i \in \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}$  for all  $i \in [\rho]$  and  $\boldsymbol{\alpha} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho\ell}$ . Compute the shifted sets  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$ . Finally, compute

$$r_i \leftarrow H\left(\prod_{j \in S_i} g^{\alpha_j t_j}\right) \text{ and } \pi_i \leftarrow \{(j, g^{t_j}, g^{u_j})\}_{j \in S_i}.$$

Output  $\sigma = (s, g^{\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{V}}), r, \text{ and } \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}.$ 

- Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma$ , i,  $r_i$ ,  $\pi_i$ ): On input crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$ , sk =  $(a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\ell'})$ ,  $\sigma = (s, g^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}})$ ,  $i \in [\rho]$ ,  $r_i \in \{0, 1\}$ , and  $\pi_i = \{(j, g^{t_j}, g^{u_j})\}_{j \in S}$  for an implicitly-defined set  $S \subseteq [\rho\ell]$ , the verification algorithm performs the following checks:
  - Compute  $(\hat{S}_1, \dots, \hat{S}_\rho, \boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(s)$  and the shifted set  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$ . It checks that  $S = S_i$  and outputs 0 if not.
  - It checks that  $g^{u_j} = (g^{t_j a})(g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_j})$  for all  $j \in S$ , and outputs 0 if not.
  - It checks that  $r_i = H(\prod_{j \in S} g^{\alpha_j t_j})$  and outputs 0 if not.

If all checks pass, the verification algorithm outputs 1.

Correctness and security analysis. We now state the correctness and security theorems for Construction 4.18 and provide the proofs in Appendix C.3.

<span id="page-27-1"></span>Theorem 4.19 (Correctness). Construction 4.18 is correct.

<span id="page-27-3"></span>Theorem 4.20 (Succinctness). Construction 4.18 is succinct.

<span id="page-27-4"></span>**Theorem 4.21** (CRS Indistinguishability). Suppose the k-Lin assumption holds for GroupGen. Then, Construction 4.18 satisfies CRS indistinguishability.

<span id="page-27-5"></span>**Theorem 4.22** (Statistical Binding in Binding Mode). Construction 4.18 satisfies statistical binding in binding mode.

<span id="page-27-2"></span>**Theorem 4.23** (Statistical Simulation in Hiding Mode). If G is a secure PRG and  $\mathcal{H}$  satisfies statistical uniformity, then Construction 4.18 satisfies statistical simulation in hiding mode against malicious verifiers.

# <span id="page-27-0"></span>5 Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generators from QR

In this section, we show how to similarly construct dual-mode hidden-bits generators from subgroup-indistinguishability-type assumptions [BG10], and specifically, from the QR and DCR assumptions. We begin in Section 5.1 with a basic construction from QR (the analog of Construction 4.3), and then show in Section 5.2 how we can use similar techniques from Section 4.3 to obtain a dual-mode hidden-bits generator with malicious security also from the QR assumption. While the construction with malicious security (Construction 5.9) subsumes the basic construction (Construction 5.3), we begin with the basic construction because it is both simpler to understand and contains all of the essential ingredients for realizing HBGs from a subgroup-indistinguishability-type assumption. Finally, in Appendix E, we show how to adapt our techniques to also obtain dual-mode HBGs from the DCR assumption.

#### <span id="page-28-0"></span>5.1 Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generator from QR

In this section, we describe our construction of a dual-mode hidden-bits generator from the quadratic residuosity (QR) assumption [GM82]. We first recall some basic notation and the QR assumption.

**Notation.** Our construction works with a modulus N = pq that is a product of safe primes p, q: namely, p = 2p' + 1 and q = 2q' + 1 for primes p' and q'. We write  $\mathbb{J}_N$  to denote the multiplicative subgroup of  $\mathbb{Z}_N^*$  with Jacobi symbol +1 (which has order 2p'q') and  $\mathbb{Q}\mathbb{R}_N$  to denote the multiplicative subgroup of quadratic residues modulo N (which has order p'q'). In the following description, we use the fact that  $\mathbb{J}_N$  splits into two subgroups of coprime order: namely,  $\mathbb{J}_N = \mathbb{Q}\mathbb{R}_N \times \mathbb{H}$ , where  $\mathbb{H}$  is the multiplicative subgroup  $\{\pm 1\}$  of order 2 generated by -1. When N is a product of safe primes,  $\mathbb{Q}\mathbb{R}_N$  is also cyclic. As such, we will write q to denote a generator of  $\mathbb{Q}\mathbb{R}_N$  and h = -1 to denote the generator of  $\mathbb{H}$ . We will typically write elements of  $\mathbb{J}_N$  with a bar (e.g.,  $\bar{c}, \bar{t}$ ). In the analysis, it will be more conducive to analyze the components in their respective subgroups. In this case, we will often express elements of  $\mathbb{J}_N$  as  $\bar{c} = g^c h^{\hat{c}} \in \mathbb{J}_N$ , for exponents  $c \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}$  and  $\hat{c} \in \mathbb{Z}_2$ .

**Definition 5.1** (Sampling Safe Prime Product Modulus). A safe prime product modulus sampler SampleModulus is an efficient algorithm that on input the security parameter  $\lambda$ , outputs a tuple (N, p, q) where N = pq, p = 2p' + 1, q = 2q' + 1, for distinct primes p', q' where  $1/p', 1/q' = \text{negl}(\lambda)$ .

**Definition 5.2** (Quadratic Residuosity Assumption [GM82]). A safe prime product sampler SampleModulus satisfies the quadratic residuosity (QR) assumption if, for any efficient adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , and any sampling  $(N, p, q) \leftarrow \mathsf{SampleModulus}(1^{\lambda}), x \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Q}\mathbb{R}_N$ , where p = 2p' + 1 and q = 2q' + 1,

$$|\mathrm{Pr}[\mathcal{A}(N,x)=1] - \mathrm{Pr}[\mathcal{A}(N,(-1)\cdot x)=1]| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Our QR-based construction is conceptually similar to the QR-based trapdoor hash function of  $[DGI^+19]$ , Section 4.3]. Like  $[DGI^+19]$ , it uses the predicate LEQ:  $\mathbb{J}_N \times \mathbb{J}_N \to \{0,1\}$  that outputs 1 if the bit representation of its first argument is no larger than that of its second argument in some lexicographical ordering. In particular, for all  $x_1, x_2 \in \mathbb{J}_N$ , it will be the case that  $LEQ(x_1, x_2) = 1 - LEQ(x_2, x_1)$ .

<span id="page-28-1"></span>Construction 5.3 (Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generator from QR). Let  $\rho$  be the output length of the hidden-bits generator. Our QR-based dual-mode hidden-bits generator (HBG) works as follows:

- Setup( $1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}$ , mode)  $\rightarrow$  crs: Sample  $(N, p, q) \leftarrow$  SampleModulus( $1^{\lambda}$ ), and let g be a generator of  $\mathbb{QR}_N$ . Let h = -1 be the generator of  $\mathbb{H} = \{\pm 1\}$ . The setup algorithm then samples a vector  $\mathbf{v} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}^{\rho}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ , scalars  $s_1, \ldots, s_{\rho} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ , and sets  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i \in \mathbb{Z}^{\rho}_2$  for  $i \in [\rho]$  as follows:
  - If mode = hiding, set  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{e}_i$  where  $\mathbf{e}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_2^{\rho}$  is the  $i^{\text{th}}$  basis vector.
  - If mode = binding, set  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{0}$ .

Finally, it sample a hash function  $H \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$ , where  $\mathcal{H}$  is a family of hash functions with domain  $\mathbb{Z}_N$  and range  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . Output  $\operatorname{crs} = (N,g,h,H,g^{\mathbf{v}},g^{s_1\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_1},\ldots,g^{s_{\rho}\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\rho}})$ .

• KeyGen(crs)  $\to$  (pk, sk): On input crs =  $(N, g, h, H, \overline{\mathbf{v}}, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_1, \dots, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_{\rho})$ , sample  $a_{\tau}, b_{\tau, i} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$  for all  $\tau \in [T]$  and  $i \in [\rho]$ , where  $T = 2(\lambda + \lceil \log N \rceil)$ . It computes and outputs the public key  $\mathsf{pk} = \{\overline{\mathbf{v}}^{b_{\tau, i}} \overline{\mathbf{w}}_i^{a_{\tau}}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]}$  and  $\mathsf{sk} = \{a_{\tau}, b_{\tau, i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]}$ .

• GenBits(crs, pk) → (σ, r, {πi}i∈[ρ] ): On input crs = (N, g, h, H, v, w1, . . . , wρ) and pk = {zτ,i}τ∈[T],i∈[ρ] , sample y <sup>r</sup><sup>←</sup> <sup>Z</sup> ρ bN/2c and compute for all τ ∈ [T] and i ∈ [ρ]

$$\overline{c} \leftarrow \prod_{j \in [\rho]} \overline{v}_j^{y_i} \quad \text{and} \quad \overline{t}_i \leftarrow \prod_{j \in [\rho]} \overline{w}_{i,j}^{y_j} \quad \text{and} \quad \overline{u}_{\tau,i} \leftarrow \prod_{j \in [\rho]} \overline{z}_{\tau,i,j}^{y_j}.$$

For each i ∈ [ρ], let u<sup>i</sup> ← H(u1,i, . . . , uT,i) and r<sup>i</sup> ← LEQ(t<sup>i</sup> ,tih). Output σ = c, r, and π = {(t<sup>i</sup> , ui)}i∈[ρ] .

• Verify(crs,sk, σ, i, r<sup>i</sup> , πi): On input crs = (N, g, h, H, v, w1, . . . , wρ), the verification key sk = {a<sup>τ</sup> , bτ,i}τ∈[T],i∈[ρ] , σ = c, i ∈ [ρ], r<sup>i</sup> ∈ {0, 1}, and π<sup>i</sup> = (t<sup>i</sup> , ui), output 1 if

<span id="page-29-4"></span>
$$u_i = H(\overline{t}_i^{a_1} \overline{c}^{b_{1,i}}, \dots, \overline{t}_i^{a_T} \overline{c}^{b_{T,i}}), \tag{5.1}$$

and r<sup>i</sup> = LEQ(t<sup>i</sup> ,tih). Otherwise, output 0.

The group elements that are input to algorithms KeyGen, GenBits, and Verify are assumed to be elements of the subgroup J<sup>N</sup> of Z ∗ <sup>N</sup> . Note that since membership in <sup>J</sup><sup>N</sup> can be efficiently tested given the modulus N (by computing the Jacobi symbol), each of the algorithms will first check this condition, and proceed only if all of the inputs are from the correct domains. We omit this explicit check in the above description for ease of exposition.

Correctness and security analysis. We now state the correctness and security theorems for Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) and give the proofs in Appendix [D.1.](#page-54-0)

<span id="page-29-2"></span>Theorem 5.4 (Correctness). Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) is correct.

<span id="page-29-5"></span>Theorem 5.5 (Succinctness). Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) is succinct.

<span id="page-29-6"></span>Theorem 5.6 (CRS Indistinguishability). Suppose the QR assumption holds with respect to SampleModulus. Then, Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) satisfies CRS indistinguishability.

<span id="page-29-7"></span>Theorem 5.7 (Statistical Binding in Binding Mode). If H is pairwise independent, Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) satisfies statistical binding in binding mode.

<span id="page-29-3"></span>Theorem 5.8 (Statistical Simulation in Hiding Mode). Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) satisfies statistical simulation in hiding mode.

### <span id="page-29-0"></span>5.2 Dual-Mode Hidden-Bits Generator with Malicious Security from QR

In this section, we show how to extend Construction [5.3](#page-28-1) to obtain a dual-mode hidden-bits generator with malicious security from the QR assumption. Our construction is conceptually similar to Construction [4.18](#page-25-1) from k-Lin.

<span id="page-29-1"></span>Construction 5.9 (Dual-Mode HBG with Malicious Security from QR). Let ρ be the output length of the hidden-bits generator. We rely on a similar set of building blocks as Construction [4.18:](#page-25-1)

• Let SampleModulus be a safe prime modulus sampler. Let T = 2(λ + dlog Ne), where dlog Ne is a bound on the bit-length of the modulus output by SampleModulus (on input 1<sup>λ</sup> ).

• Let  $\ell = 36\rho\lambda^2T$  and define  $\mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell} := \{S \subseteq [\ell] : |S| = \lambda\}$  to be the set of all subsets of  $[\ell]$  that contains exactly  $\lambda$  elements. Let  $G : \{0,1\}^{\kappa} \to \mathcal{T}^{\rho}_{\lambda,\ell} \times \mathbb{Z}^{\rho\ell}_2$  be a PRG with seed length  $\kappa = \kappa(\lambda)$ . Here, N is the modulus output by SampleModulus. We refer to Construction 4.18 for a description of how to construct such a PRG.

We construct the dual-mode designated-verifier HBG with malicious security as follows:

- Setup $(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \text{mode}) \to \text{crs: Let } \ell' = \rho \ell$ . Sample  $(N, p, q) \leftarrow \text{SampleModulus}(1^{\lambda})$ . Let g be a generator of  $\mathbb{QR}_N$  and h = -1 be the generator of  $\mathbb{H} = \{\pm 1\}$ . The setup algorithm samples a vector  $\mathbf{v} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}^{\ell'}$ , scalars  $s_1, \ldots, s_{\ell'} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ , and sets  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_2^{\ell'}$  for  $i \in [\ell']$  as follows:
  - If mode = hiding, set  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{e}_i$  where  $\mathbf{e}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_2^{\ell'}$  is the  $i^{\text{th}}$  basis vector.
  - If mode = binding, set  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{0}$ .

Finally, it sample a hash function  $H \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$ , where  $\mathcal{H}$  is a family of hash functions with domain  $\mathbb{Z}_N$  and range  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . Output  $\operatorname{crs} = (N,g,h,H,g^{\mathbf{v}},g^{s_1\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_1},\ldots,g^{s_{\ell'}\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\ell'}})$ .

- KeyGen(crs)  $\rightarrow$  (pk, sk): On input crs =  $(N, g, h, H, \overline{\mathbf{v}}, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_1, \dots, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_{\ell'})$ , sample  $a_{\tau}, b_{\tau,i} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$  for all  $\tau \in [T]$  and  $i \in [\ell']$ . It computes and outputs the public key  $\mathsf{pk} = \{\overline{\mathbf{v}}^{b_{\tau,i}} \overline{\mathbf{w}}_i^{a_{\tau}}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\ell']}$  and  $\mathsf{sk} = \{a_{\tau}, b_{\tau,i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\ell']}$ .
- GenBits(crs, pk)  $\to$   $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]})$ : On input crs  $= (N, g, h, H, \overline{\mathbf{v}}, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_1, \dots, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_{\ell'})$  and pk  $= \{\overline{\mathbf{z}}_{\tau,i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\ell']}$ , sample  $\mathbf{y} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}^{\ell'}$  and compute for all  $\tau \in [T]$  and  $i \in [\ell']$

$$\overline{c} \leftarrow \prod_{j \in [\ell']} \overline{v}_j^{y_i} \quad \text{and} \quad \overline{t}_i \leftarrow \prod_{j \in [\ell']} \overline{w}_{i,j}^{y_j} \quad \text{and} \quad \overline{u}_{\tau,i} \leftarrow \prod_{j \in [\ell']} \overline{z}_{\tau,i,j}^{y_j}.$$

In addition, for each  $i \in [\ell']$ , compute  $u_i \leftarrow H(\overline{u}_{1,i}, \dots, \overline{u}_{T,i})$ . Next, sample a PRG seed  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\kappa}$  and compute  $(\hat{S}_1, \dots, \hat{S}_{\rho}, \boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(s)$  where  $\hat{S}_i \in \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}$  for all  $i \in [\rho]$  and  $\boldsymbol{\alpha} \in \mathbb{Z}_2^{\rho\ell}$ . For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , compute the shifted sets  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$  and set

$$\overline{t}_i' \leftarrow \prod_{j \in S_i} \overline{t}_j^{\alpha_j} \quad \text{and} \quad r_i \leftarrow \mathsf{LEQ}(\overline{t}_i', \overline{t}_i'h) \quad \text{and} \quad \pi_i \leftarrow \{(j, \overline{t}_j, u_j)\}_{j \in S_i}.$$

Output  $\sigma = (\mathsf{s}, \overline{c}), r, \text{ and } \pi = \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}.$ 

- Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma$ , i,  $r_i$ ,  $\pi_i$ ): On input crs =  $(N, g, h, H, \overline{\mathbf{v}}, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_1, \dots, \overline{\mathbf{w}}_{\ell'})$ , sk =  $\{a_{\tau}, b_{\tau,i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\ell']}$ ,  $\sigma = (\mathbf{s}, \overline{c}), i \in [\rho], r_i \in \{0, 1\}$ , and  $\pi_i = \{(h, \overline{t}_j, u_j)\}_{j \in S}$  for an implicitly-defined set  $S \subseteq [\ell']$ , the verification algorithm performs the following checks:
  - Compute  $(\hat{S}_1, \dots, \hat{S}_\rho, \boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(s)$  and the shifted set  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$ . It checks that  $S = S_i$  and outputs 0 if not.
  - It checks that

$$u_j = H(\bar{t}_j^{a_1} \bar{c}^{b_{1,j}}, \dots, \bar{t}_j^{a_T} \bar{c}^{b_{T,j}}),$$
 (5.2)

for all  $j \in S$ , and outputs 0 if not.

– It computes  $\bar{t}_i' \leftarrow \prod_{j \in S} \bar{t}_j^{\alpha_j}$  and checks that  $r_i = \mathsf{LEQ}(\bar{t}_i', \bar{t}_i'h)$ .

The group elements that are input to algorithms KeyGen, GenBits, and Verify are assumed to be elements of the subgroup  $\mathbb{J}_N$  of  $\mathbb{Z}_N^*$ . Note that since membership in  $\mathbb{J}_N$  can be efficiently tested given the modulus N (by computing the Jacobi symbol), each of the algorithms will first check this condition, and proceed *only if* all of the inputs are from the correct domains. We omit this explicit check in the above description for ease of exposition.

Correctness and security analysis. We now state the correctness and security theorems for Construction 5.9 and give the proofs in Appendix D.2.

<span id="page-31-2"></span>**Theorem 5.10** (Correctness). Construction 5.9 is correct.

<span id="page-31-4"></span>Theorem 5.11 (Succinctness). Construction 5.9 is succinct.

<span id="page-31-5"></span>**Theorem 5.12** (CRS Indistinguishability). Suppose the QR assumption holds with respect to SampleModulus. Then, Construction 5.9 satisfies CRS indistinguishability.

<span id="page-31-6"></span>**Theorem 5.13** (Statistical Binding in Binding Mode). If  $\mathcal{H}$  is pairwise independent, Construction 5.9 satisfies statistical binding in binding mode.

<span id="page-31-3"></span>**Theorem 5.14** (Statistical Simulation in Hiding Mode). Construction 5.9 satisfies statistical simulation in hiding mode.

### <span id="page-31-0"></span>6 Instantiations and Extensions

In this section, we provide the main implications of our framework for constructing statistical (and more generally, dual-mode) NIZKs. We conclude by describing two simple extensions to augment our NIZKs with additional properties.

**Dual-mode MDV-NIZKs.** By instantiating Construction 3.4 with a dual-mode MDV hidden-bits generator (e.g., Constructions 4.18, 5.9 and E.16), we obtain a dual-mode MDV-NIZK (Theorems 3.5, 3.7 and 3.11). We summarize our instantiations with the following corollaries:

Corollary 6.1 (Dual-Mode MDV-NIZK from k-Lin). Under the k-Lin assumption over pairing-free groups (for any  $k \geq 1$ ), there exists a statistical MDV-NIZK argument (with non-adaptive soundness) in the common random string model, and a computational MDV-NIZK proof (with adaptive soundness) for NP in the common reference string model.

**Corollary 6.2** (Dual-Mode MDV-NIZK from QR or DCR). Under the QR or DCR assumptions, there exists a statistical MDV-NIZK argument (with non-adaptive soundness) and a computational MDV-NIZK proof (with adaptive soundness) for NP in the common reference string model.

<span id="page-31-1"></span>Remark 6.3 (Perfect Zero-Knowledge DV-NIZK from k-Lin). As discussed in Remark C.8, Construction 4.3 satisfies perfect statistical simulation if the hash function  $H: \mathbb{G} \setminus \{g^0\} \to \{0,1\}$  is perfectly uniform and there is an efficient algorithm to exactly sample  $t \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that  $H(g^t) = r$  for any  $r \in \{0,1\}$ . Here we describe a straightforward candidate for H when  $\mathbb{G} = E(\mathbb{F}_p)$  is an elliptic-curve group of prime-order. We can write  $E(\mathbb{F}_p) = \{y^2 = x^3 + Ax + B \mid (x,y) \in \mathbb{F}_p^2\} \cup \{\mathcal{O}\}$ , where  $\mathcal{O}$  is the identity element. For a point  $(x,y) \in \mathbb{G} \setminus \{\mathcal{O}\}$ , we define the hash function H(x,y) := sign(y), where sign(y) outputs 0 if  $y \in \mathbb{F}_p$  is lexicographically smaller than  $-y \in \mathbb{F}_p$ , and 1 otherwise. Since

 $(x,y) \in \mathbb{G}$  implies that  $(x,-y) \in \mathbb{G}$ , H perfectly partitions  $\mathbb{G} \setminus \{\mathcal{O}\}$  into two equal-size sets, and correspondingly, H is perfectly uniform. Next, it is straightforward to sample  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{G} \setminus \{\mathcal{O}\}$  such that H(t) = r. Simply sample  $(x,y) \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{G} \setminus \{\mathcal{O}\}$ , and output either (x,y) or (x,-y), depending on r. Thus,  $\mathcal{H}$  satisfies the required properties and combined with Construction 4.3, gives a hidden-bits generator with perfect simulation in hiding mode. Combined with Construction 3.4, we obtain a perfect DV-NIZK argument from the k-Lin assumption in pairing-free groups. Note that our MDV-NIZK construction from the k-Lin assumption (Construction 4.18) does not satisfy perfect simulation in hiding mode, so we do not obtain a perfect MDV-NIZK argument.

**Publicly-verifiable statistical NIZK arguments.** By instantiating Construction B.1 with a publicly-verifiable hidden-bits generator satisfying statistical simulation (e.g., Construction 4.13), we obtain a publicly-verifiable statistical NIZK argument in the common reference string model:

Corollary 6.4 (Publicly-Verifiable Statistical NIZK Argument from Pairings). Suppose that the k-Lin assumption holds in  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and the k-KerLin assumption holds in  $\mathbb{G}_2$  (for any  $k \geq 1$ ) over a pairing group. Then, there exists a publicly-verifiable statistical NIZK argument for NP (with non-adaptive soundness) in the common reference string model.

**Extensions.** We conclude by describing two simple extensions to our framework to support languages where statements can have an arbitrary a priori unbounded polynomial length as well as how to obtain a proof of knowledge.

<span id="page-32-0"></span>Remark 6.5 (Unbounded Statement Size). All of our NIZK constructions in this paper assumed an a priori bound on the length n of the statements in the language (which determines the length of the hidden-bits string we require), and the length of the CRS scales with n. Using the same idea from [QRW19, §7], it is straightforward to adapt the construction to have a fixed-size CRS (independent of n) that supports proving statements of arbitrary  $poly(\lambda)$  size. We require a noninteractive commitment in the CRS model, and we will use 3-SAT as our underlying NP-complete language. To prove that a specific 3-CNF formula is satisfiable, the prover would commit to the satisfying assignment (one variable at a time), and then use the NIZK to prove that the committed values indeed satisfy each clause. Observe that we now only require a NIZK that supports a fixed-size language to implement this transformation. Moreover, if we instantiate the commitment with a "dual-mode" commitment, where in one mode, the commitment is statistically binding, and in the other, is statistically hiding, then we retain the dual-mode properties of the underlying NIZK. We can build dual-mode commitments from any lossy public-key encryption scheme [BHY09] (implied by standard intractability assumptions like DDH, QR, and DCR). Specifically, the CRS would contain a public key for the encryption scheme, and a commitment to a bit  $b \in \{0,1\}$  would be an encryption of b with fresh randomness r. The randomness r then serves as the commitment opening. When the lossy encryption scheme is injective, then the commitment is statistically binding and if the encryption scheme is lossy, then the commitment scheme is statistically hiding.

Remark 6.6 (Proofs of Knowledge). It is also straightforward to update our NIZK constructions to obtain a proof of knowledge via the classic transformation where the prover encrypts the witness (using a public-key encryption scheme) and gives a proof that the encrypted witness satisfies the relation [DP92]. To preserve the dual-mode properties of the NIZK, we again require a lossy public-key encryption scheme [BHY09] (similar to Remark 6.5).

# <span id="page-36-4"></span>A Analysis of Construction 3.4 (Dual-Mode DV-NIZK)

In this section, we provide complete proofs for statements in Section 3.1.

**Proof of Theorem 3.7 (Statistical Soundness).** The argument proceeds very similarly to the corresponding proof of adaptive statistical soundness from [QRW19]. We use a simple hybrid argument:

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the real soundness experiment where the challenger begins by sampling  $\operatorname{crs} \leftarrow \operatorname{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^\lambda)$  and  $(\operatorname{\mathsf{pk}},\operatorname{\mathsf{sk}}) \leftarrow \operatorname{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}})$ , where  $\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}} = (\lambda,s,\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}}_{\operatorname{\mathsf{HBG}}})$ ,  $\operatorname{\mathsf{pk}} = \operatorname{\mathsf{pk}}_{\operatorname{\mathsf{HBG}}}$ , and  $\operatorname{\mathsf{sk}} = \operatorname{\mathsf{sk}}_{\operatorname{\mathsf{HBG}}}$ . The challenger gives  $\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}}$  and  $\operatorname{\mathsf{pk}}$  to  $\mathcal A$ . Adversary  $\mathcal A$  can then make verification queries on pairs  $(x,\pi)$  and the challenger responds with  $\operatorname{\mathsf{Verify}}(\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}},\operatorname{\mathsf{sk}},x,\pi)$ . At the end of the experiment, the adversary outputs  $(x^*,\pi^*)$  and the output of the experiment is 1 if  $x^* \notin \mathcal L$  and  $\operatorname{\mathsf{Verify}}(\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}},\operatorname{\mathsf{sk}},x^*,\pi^*)=1$ .
- Hyb<sub>1</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>0</sub> except that, at the end of the experiment, after the adversary outputs its statement  $x^* \in \{0,1\}^n$  and proof  $\pi^* = (\sigma^*, I^*, r_{I^*}^*, \{\pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}^*\}_{i \in I^*}, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}^*)$ , the challenger performs the following additional check:
  - Compute  $r \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma^*)$ . If  $\mathsf{HBG.Verify}(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma^*, i, r_i^*, \pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}^*) = 1$  for all  $i \in I^*$  and  $r_{I^*} \neq r_{I^*}^*$ , then the challenger aborts the experiments and outputs  $\bot$ .

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output distribution of an execution of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) \stackrel{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  and that  $\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) = 1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma A.1.** If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  is statistically binding in binding mode, then for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* The only difference between  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is the additional check the challenger performs when computing  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{sk},x^*,\pi^*)$  which precisely coincides with the binding property of the hidden-bits generator. An adversary that causes  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  to output  $\bot$  with noticeable probability can break the binding property with the same probability (formally, the reduction algorithm can simulate the  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{sk},\cdot,\cdot)$  queries via oracle access to  $\mathsf{HBG}.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}},\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{HBG}},\cdot,\cdot,\cdot,\cdot)$  in the binding game).

<span id="page-36-5"></span>**Lemma A.2.** For all adversaries A,  $Pr[Hyb_1(A) = 1] = negl(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* Let  $x^* \in \{0,1\}^n$  and  $\pi^* = (\sigma^*, I^*, r_{I^*}^*, \{\pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}^*\}_{i \in I^*}, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}^*)$  be the adversary's output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . For  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  to output 1, the following conditions must hold:

- $r_{I^*} = r_{I^*}^*$  where  $r \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma^*)$ ;
- HBM.Verify $(1^{\lambda}, I^*, r_{I^*} \oplus s_{I^*}, x^*, \pi^*_{\mathsf{HBM}}) = 1.$

Fix any commitment string  $\sigma \in \{0,1\}^{\ell}$  and let  $r \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma)$  be the associated sequence of bits. For a randomly and independently sampled  $s \stackrel{\mathsf{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\rho}$ ,  $r_{I^*} \oplus s_{I^*}$  is also uniformly random. By soundness of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{HBM}.\mathsf{Verify}(1^{\lambda},I^*,r_{I^*}\oplus s_{I^*},x^*,\pi^*_{\mathsf{HBM}})]=\varepsilon(\lambda).$$

Taking a union bound over all possible commitments  $\sigma \in \{0,1\}^{\ell}$ , we have that

$$\Pr[\exists \, \sigma \in \{0,1\}^{\ell} : \mathsf{HBM.Verify}(1^{\lambda}, I^*, r_{I^*} \oplus s_{I^*}, x^*, \pi^*_{\mathsf{HBM}})] \leq 2^{\ell} \cdot \varepsilon(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where in the above expression,  $r \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{crs}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma^*)$ . Thus,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  outputs 1 with negligible probability.

Since the distributions  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  are statistically indistinguishable, the probability that  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})$  outputs 1 (i.e.,  $\mathcal{A}$  breaks soundness) is negligible.

**Proof of Theorem 3.11 (Statistical Zero-Knowledge).** The proof follows by essentially the same argument as the proof of Theorem 3.8. Specifically, let  $\mathcal{S}_{HBM}$  be the zero-knowledge simulator for  $\Pi_{HBM}$  and  $\mathcal{S}_{HBG} = (\mathcal{S}_{HBG,1}, \mathcal{S}_{HBG,2})$  be the simulator for  $\Pi_{HBG}$  in hiding mode. We construct the the zero-knowledge simulator  $\mathcal{S} = (\mathcal{S}_1, \mathcal{S}_2)$  for  $\Pi_{dvNIZK}$  as follows:

- $S_1(1^{\lambda}) \to (\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}})$ : Run  $(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}) \leftarrow S_{\mathsf{HBG}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$  and sample  $\widetilde{s} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}^{\rho}$ . Output  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = \mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  and  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\lambda, s, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}_{\mathsf{HBG}})$ .
- $S_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}},\mathsf{pk},x) \to \widetilde{\pi}$ : On input  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = \mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}}$ ,  $\mathsf{pk}$  and  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$ , run  $(\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}},\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}}) \leftarrow S_{\mathsf{HBM}}(1^{\lambda},x)$  and  $(\widetilde{\sigma},\{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i\in\widetilde{I}}) \leftarrow S_{\mathsf{HBG},2}(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{HBG}},\mathsf{pk},\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}} \oplus \widetilde{s}_{\widetilde{I}})$ . Output the simulated proof  $\widetilde{\pi} = (\widetilde{\sigma},\widetilde{I},\widetilde{r}_{\widetilde{I}} \oplus \widetilde{s}_{\widetilde{I}},\{\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBG},i}\}_{i\in\widetilde{I}},\widetilde{\pi}_{\mathsf{HBM}})$ .

To complete the proof, we use the same hybrid structure as in the proof of Theorem 3.8:

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the real distribution.
- $Hyb_1$ : Same as  $Hyb_0$ , except the challenger uses  $\mathcal{S}_{HBG,1}$  to generate the common reference string. It uses  $\mathcal{S}_{HBG,2}$  to simulate the openings to the hidden-bits generator when responding to oracle queries.
- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub>, except when responding to oracle queries, the challenger uses the simulator for the hidden-bits model NIZK to simulate the proofs. This is the simulated distribution.

By essentially the same argument as in the proof of Lemma 3.9,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  are statistically indistinguishable if  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  satisfies statistical simulation for malicious keys. Similarly,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  are statistically indistinguishable if  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  provides statistical zero-knowledge. This follows by an identical argument as in the proof of Lemma 3.10.

## <span id="page-38-0"></span>B Publicly-Verifiable Statistical NIZK Arguments from HBG

In this section, we show that a (publicly-verifiable) hidden-bits generator that satisfies computational binding (Definition [4.10\)](#page-23-1) yields a (publicly-verifiable NIZK) that satisfies non-adaptive computational soundness. The analysis is very similar to the proof of statistical soundness (Theorem [3.7\)](#page-19-0). Here, we consider a variant of Construction [3.4](#page-18-1) that is publicly-verifiable but has just a single mode. This is essentially the construction from [\[QRW19\]](#page-36-0). For completeness, we recall the specific construction:

<span id="page-38-1"></span>Construction B.1 (NIZK from Publicly-Verifiable HBG). Let L ⊆ {0, 1} <sup>n</sup> be an NP language with associated NP relation R. We rely on the following building blocks:

- Let ΠHBG = (HBG.Setup, HBG.GenBits, HBG.Verify) be a publicly-verifiable hidden-bits generator with commitments of length ` = `(λ, ρ), where λ is the security parameter and ρ is the output length of the generator.
- Let ΠHBM = (HBM.Prove, HBM.Verify) be a NIZK in the hidden-bits model for L, and let ρ = ρ(λ) be the length of the hidden-bits string for ΠHBM.

We construct a publicly-verifiable NIZK ΠNIZK = (Setup, Prove, Verify) for L as follows:

- Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> ) → crs: On input λ ∈ N, sample s <sup>r</sup>← {0, <sup>1</sup>} ρ . Run crsHBG ← HBG.Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 ρ , mode) and output crs = (λ, s, crsHBG).
- Prove(crs, x, w) → π: On input crs = (λ, s, crsHBG), x ∈ {0, 1} n , and w, compute a hidden-bits string (σ, r, {πHBG,i}i∈{1,...,ρ} ) ← HBG.GenBits(crsHBG, pkHBG), and an HBM proof (I, πHBM) ← HBM.Prove(1<sup>λ</sup> , r ⊕ s, x, w). Output π = (σ, I, r<sup>I</sup> , {πHBG,i}i∈<sup>I</sup> , πHBM).
- Verify(crs, x, π): On input crs = (λ, s, crsHBG), x ∈ {0, 1} n , and π = (σ, I, r<sup>I</sup> , {πHBG,i}i∈<sup>I</sup> , πHBM), output 1 if HBM.Verify(1<sup>λ</sup> , I, r<sup>I</sup> ⊕ s<sup>I</sup> , x, πHBM) = 1 and HBG.Verify(crsHBG, σ, i, r<sup>i</sup> , πHBG,i) = 1 for all i ∈ I. Otherwise, output 0.

Theorem B.2 (Completeness). If ΠHBM is complete and ΠHBG is correct, then ΠNIZK from Construction [B.1](#page-38-1) is complete.

Proof. Follows by a similar argument as the proof of Theorem [3.5.](#page-19-2)

Theorem B.3 (Computational Soundness). If ΠHBM is statistically sound with soundness error ε(λ), ΠHBG is is computationally binding (Definition [4.10\)](#page-23-1), and 2 ` · ε = negl(λ), then ΠNIZK from Construction [B.1](#page-38-1) satisfies non-adaptive computational soundness.

Proof. The proof uses a similar hybrid structure as in the proof of Theorem [3.7,](#page-19-0) except we are in the non-adaptive setting. In particular, fix a statement x /∈ L, and let E be the extractor associated with ΠHBG (from Definition [4.10\)](#page-23-1). Consider the following sequence of hybrid experiments:

- Hyb<sup>0</sup> : This is the real soundness experiment, where the challenger begins by sampling crs ← Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> ) and gives crs = (λ, s, crsHBG) to A. At the end of the experiment, A outputs a proof π <sup>∗</sup> and the output of the experiment is 1 if Verify(crs, x, π<sup>∗</sup> ) = 1.
- Hyb<sup>1</sup> : Same as Hyb<sup>0</sup> , except the challenger samples (st<sup>E</sup> , crs <sup>f</sup>HBG) ← E1(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 ρ ) and uses crs <sup>f</sup> = (λ, s, crs <sup>f</sup>HBG) in place of crs.

- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub>, except at the end of the experiment, after the adversary outputs  $\pi^* = (\sigma^*, I^*, r_{I^*}^*, \{\pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}^*\}_{i \in I^*}, \pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}^*)$ , the challenger performs the following check:
  - Compute  $r \leftarrow \mathcal{E}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \sigma^*)$ . If HBG.Verify $(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma^*, i, r_i^*, \pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}^*) = 1$  for all  $i \in I^*$  and  $r_{I^*} \neq r_{I^*}^*$ , then the challenger aborts the experiments and outputs  $\bot$ .

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output distribution of an execution of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that each adjacent pair of hybrids are computationally indistinguishable, and moreover, that  $\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) = 1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma B.4.** If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  satisfies computational binding, then for all efficient  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) \overset{c}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* Follows immediately by the CRS indistinguishability property of  $\mathcal{E}$ .

**Lemma B.5.** If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  satisfies computational binding, then for all efficient  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) \overset{c}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* The only difference between  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is the additional check the challenger performs when computing  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, x, \pi^*)$ . But if  $\mathcal{A}$  manages to output a commitment  $\sigma^*$  and an index  $i \in [\rho]$  such that  $\mathsf{HBG}.\mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}_{\mathsf{HBG}}, \sigma^*, i, r_i^*, \pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i}^*) = 1$  and  $r_i \neq r_i^*$  where  $r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{E}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \sigma^*)$ , then  $(\sigma^*, i, r_i^*, \pi_{\mathsf{HBG},i})$  breaks the binding property of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$ . Hence, if  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  is computationally binding, then the probability that the additional check fails and the challenger aborts is negligible.

**Lemma B.6.** For all adversaries A,  $Pr[Hyb_2(A) = 1] = negl(\lambda)$ .

*Proof.* Follows by a similar argument as the proof of Lemma A.2.

Thus, for all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) \overset{c}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$  and  $\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) = 1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Thus, the probability that  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})$  outputs 1 (meaning that  $\mathcal{A}$  breaks non-adaptive soundness) is also negligible.

**Theorem B.7** (Statistical Zero-Knowledge). If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBM}}$  satisfies statistical zero-knowledge and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  provides statistical simulation, then  $\Pi_{\mathsf{NIZK}}$  from Construction B.1 is statistical zero-knowledge.

*Proof.* Follows by a similar argument as the proof of Theorem 3.8.  $\hfill\Box$ 

# C Analysis of Constructions from k-Lin (Section 4)

In this section, we provide the analysis of the dual-mode hidden-bits generators from Section 4.

# <span id="page-39-0"></span>C.1 Analysis of Construction 4.3 (Dual-Mode HBG from k-Lin)

In this section, we give the proofs for the correctness and security theorems (Theorems 4.4 to 4.8) for the dual-mode hidden-bits generator from the k-Lin assumption (Construction 4.3).

**Proof of Theorem 4.4 (Correctness).** Fix  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , a polynomial  $\rho = \rho(\lambda)$ , an index  $i \in [\rho]$ , and a mode mode  $\in$  {binding, hiding}. Let  $\operatorname{crs} \leftarrow \operatorname{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \operatorname{mode})$ ,  $(\operatorname{pk}, \operatorname{sk}) \leftarrow \operatorname{KeyGen}(\operatorname{crs})$ , and  $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}) \leftarrow \operatorname{GenBits}(\operatorname{crs}, \operatorname{pk})$ . By construction,  $\operatorname{crs} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\rho}})$ ,  $\operatorname{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\rho}})$ , and  $\operatorname{sk} = (a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\rho})$ . Moreover,  $\mathbf{z}_i = \mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i$ . In addition,  $\sigma = g^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}} = g^{\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{V}}$ ,  $r_i = H(g^{t_i})$ , and  $\pi_i = (g^{t_i}, g^{u_i})$ , where  $t_i = \mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{w}_i$  and  $u_i = \mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{z}_i$  for some  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ . This means that

$$u_i = \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}_i = \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} (\mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i) = t_i a + \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_i,$$

and the verification algorithm accepts.

**Proof of Theorem 4.5 (Succinctness).** The length of a commitment in Construction 4.3 consists of k = O(1) group elements, each of which can be represented by a string of length  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ . Thus,  $|\sigma| = k \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda) = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ .

**Proof of Theorem 4.6 (CRS Indistinguishability).** Our analysis relies on the following corollary of the k-Lin assumption, which roughly says that an encoding of a random rank-k matrix is computationally indistinguishable for an encoding of a uniformly random matrix.

<span id="page-40-0"></span>**Lemma C.1** ([EHK<sup>+</sup>13]). Suppose GroupGen is a prime-order group generator where k-Lin holds. Then for all polynomials  $n = n(\lambda)$  where  $k \le n$ , the following two distributions are computationally indistinguishable:

$$(\mathcal{G}, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}}) \stackrel{c}{\approx} (\mathcal{G}, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}}),$$

 $where \ \mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow \mathsf{GroupGen}(1^{\lambda}), \ \mathbf{V} \overset{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{n \times k}, \ \mathbf{s} \overset{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k \ and \ \mathbf{w} \overset{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^n.$ 

To complete the proof observe that the output of  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^\rho, \mathsf{mode})$  for  $\mathsf{mode} \in \{\mathsf{binding}, \mathsf{hiding}\}$  differ only in how the vectors  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_\rho$  are sampled. In both cases,  $\mathsf{Setup}$  starts by sampling  $\mathbf{V} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times k}$ . Then, if  $\mathsf{mode} = \mathsf{binding}, \mathbf{w}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$  for  $\mathbf{s}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , while if  $\mathsf{mode} = \mathsf{hiding}, \mathbf{w}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ . By Lemma C.1, these two distributions are computationally indistinguishable, and the claim now follows by a standard hybrid argument.

**Proof of Theorem 4.7 (Statistical Binding).** We first define the (inefficient) Open algorithm as follows:

• Open(crs,  $\sigma$ )  $\to r$ : On input a crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\rho}})$  and a commitment  $\sigma = g^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}}$ , the open algorithm first recovers  $\mathbf{V}, \mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\rho}$  by solving the discrete logarithm problem over  $\mathbb{G}$ . For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , it checks that  $\mathbf{w}_i = \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$  for some  $\mathbf{s}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and outputs  $\bot$  if not. If all checks pass, it computes  $r_i \leftarrow H(g^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{s}_i})$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$  and outputs r.

To complete the proof, we use a hybrid argument:

•  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ : This is the real binding experiment. Namely, the challenger starts by sampling  $\mathsf{crs} \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^\rho, \mathsf{binding})$  and  $(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{crs})$ , and gives  $(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{pk})$  to the adversary. By construction,

$$\mathsf{crs} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_\rho}) \quad \text{and} \quad \mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_\rho}),$$

where  $\mathbf{z}_i = \mathbf{V}(\mathbf{s}_i a + \mathbf{b}_i)$ . The adversary can then make queries to the verification oracle. On each query  $(\sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i)$ , the challenger replies with  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{sk}, \sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i)$ . At the end of the game, the adversary outputs a tuple  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*)$ . The output of the experiment is 1 if  $r^* \neq r_i$  where  $r \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{crs}, \sigma^*)$  and  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{sk}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1$ .

- $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ , except the challenger samples  $\mathbf{x}_1, \dots, \mathbf{x}_\rho \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and computes  $\mathbf{z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{x}_i$ . It then samples  $a \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and sets  $\mathbf{b}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{x}_i \mathbf{s}_i a$ .
- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub>, except the challenger implements Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma$ , i,  $r_i$ ,  $\pi_i$ ) by computing Verify\* $(\sigma, r_i, \pi_i, \mathbf{s}_i, \mathbf{x}_i)$ , where on input  $\sigma = g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}$ ,  $r_i \in \{0, 1\}$ ,  $\pi_i = (g^{t_i}, g^{u_i})$ ,  $\mathbf{s}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ ,  $\mathbf{x}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , Verify\* $(\sigma, \pi_i, r_i, \mathbf{s}_i, \mathbf{x}_i)$  outputs 1 if and only if

$$r_i = H(g^{t_i})$$
 and  $t_i = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_i$  and  $u_i = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}_i$ .

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We show that the output distributions of each adjacent pair of hybrid experiments are statistically indistinguishable, and moreover,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$  output 0 with overwhelming probability for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ .

**Lemma C.2.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(A) \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_1(A)$ .

*Proof.* Follows by construction.

<span id="page-41-0"></span>**Lemma C.3.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(A) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_2(A)$ .

Proof. We use a hybrid argument. The challenger evaluates Verify at most Q+1 times, where  $Q=\operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$  is the bound on the number of queries the adversary makes (the challenger evaluates Verify also at the end to determine the output of the experiment).
