We define a sequence of intermediate hybrids  $\operatorname{Hyb}_{1,j}$  for  $j\in\{0,\ldots Q+1\}$ , where in hybrid  $\operatorname{Hyb}_{1,j}$ , the first j queries are handled according to the specification in  $\operatorname{Hyb}_2$  while the remaining queries are handled according to the specification in  $\operatorname{Hyb}_1$ . By construction,  $\operatorname{Hyb}_1 \equiv \operatorname{Hyb}_{1,0}$  and  $\operatorname{Hyb}_2 \equiv \operatorname{Hyb}_{1,Q+1}$ . Consider  $\operatorname{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  and  $\operatorname{Hyb}_{1,j}$  for  $j\in[Q+1]$ . These two experiments only differ in how the challenger computes the output for the  $j^{\text{th}}$  Verify call. Let  $(\operatorname{crs},\operatorname{sk},\sigma,i,r_i,\pi_i)$  be the arguments to the  $j^{\text{th}}$  Verify call, where  $\sigma=g^{\mathbf{c}^T}$  and  $\pi_i=(g^{t_i},g^{u_i})$ . Consider the behavior in  $\operatorname{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$ . Here, the verifier accepts only if

$$r_i = H(g^{t_i})$$
 and  $u_i = t_i a + \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_i = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} (\mathbf{s}_i a + \mathbf{b}_i) + (t_i - \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_i) a = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}_i + (t_i - \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_i) a$ .

We consider two possibilities:

- If  $t_i = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_i$ , then Verify in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  outputs 1 if and only if  $r_i = H(g^{t_i})$  and  $u_i = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}_i$ . This is identical to the behavior of  $\mathsf{Verify}^*$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j}$ .
- If  $t_i \neq \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_i$ , then the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  is 1 only if  $a = (u_i \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}_i)(t_i \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_i)^{-1}$ . In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$ , neither the public parameters (crs and pk) nor the responses to the first j-1 queries depend on the value of a (in particular,  $\mathsf{Verify}^*$  is independent of a, and thus, leaks no information about a). Thus, the challenger can sample  $a \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  after the adversary has chosen  $\mathbf{c}$ ,  $t_i$ , and  $u_i$ . In this case,  $\mathsf{Verify}$  outputs 1 with probability  $1/p = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Thus, with overwhelming probability, the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  is 0. This is the output of  $\mathsf{Verify}^*$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j}$ .

Since the outputs of Verify in Hyb<sub>1</sub> and Verify\* in Hyb<sub>2</sub> are statistically indistinguishable for each query, the claim now follows by a hybrid argument. To conclude the proof, it suffices to show that the output in Hyb<sub>2</sub> is always 0. Let  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*)$  be the adversary's output in Hyb<sub>2</sub>. The output in Hyb<sub>2</sub> is 1 only if Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*$ ) = 1 and  $r_{i^*} \neq r^*$  where  $r \leftarrow \text{Open}(\text{crs}, \sigma^*)$ . Write  $\sigma^* = g^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$  and  $\pi^* = (g^{t_i^*}, g^{u_i^*})$ . By definition of Open,  $r_{i^*} = H(g^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{s}_i)$ . In Hyb<sub>3</sub>, the Verify function outputs 1 only if  $t_i^* = (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s}_i$  and

$$r^* = H(g^{t_i^*}) = H(g^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{s}_i) = r_{i^*}.$$

But then, the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is 0. Since the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  are statistically indistinguishable, this means that the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  is also 0 with overwhelming probability.

Theorem 4.7 now follows by a hybrid argument.

**Proof of Theorem 4.8 (Statistical Simulation).** We construct a simulator  $\mathcal{S} = (\mathcal{S}_1, \mathcal{S}_2)$  as follows:

•  $S_1(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}) \to (\operatorname{st}_{S}, \widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{sk}})$ : Sample  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow \operatorname{GroupGen}(1^{\lambda}), H \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}, \mathbf{V} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k) \times k},$  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\rho} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}, a \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p, \text{ and } \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\rho} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k.$  For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , let  $\mathbf{z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i$ . In the following, we will write

<span id="page-42-0"></span>
$$\mathbf{W} = [\mathbf{w}_1 \mid \cdots \mid \mathbf{w}_{\rho} \mid \mathbf{V}] \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times(\rho+k)}. \tag{C.1}$$

Output  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\rho}}), \ \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\rho}}), \ \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}} = (a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_{\rho}), \ \mathrm{and} \ \mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = (\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}, \mathbf{W})$ 

•  $S_2(\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, I, r_I) \to (\widetilde{\sigma}, \{\widetilde{\pi}_i\}_{i \in I})$ : On input  $\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = (\widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}, \mathbf{W})$  where

$$\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\rho}}) \quad \text{and} \quad \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\rho}}),$$

a set of indices  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ , and a bitstring  $r_I \in \{0,1\}^{|I|}$ , the simulator samples a vector  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho}$  as follows:

- For each  $i \in I$ , sample  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  conditioned on  $H(g^{t_i}) = r_i$ . Specifically, the simulator repeatedly samples  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  until finding one that satisfies  $H(g^{t_i}) = r_i$ . If no such  $t_i$  is found after  $\lambda$  iterations, then the simulator aborts and outputs  $\perp$ .
- For the remaining indices  $i \in [\rho] \setminus I$ , sample  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ .

Next, it samples  $\mathbf{c} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and sets  $\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow [\mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mid \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}] \cdot \mathbf{W}^{-1}$  (outputting  $\bot$  if  $\mathbf{W}$  is not invertible). It computes  $\widetilde{\sigma} = g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}$  and  $g^{u_i} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{z}_i$  for each  $i \in I$ . It output  $\widetilde{\sigma}$  and  $\{\widetilde{\pi}_i\}_{i \in I}$  where  $\widetilde{\pi}_i = (g^{t_i}, g^{u_i})$ .

To show that  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{A},0]$  and  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{A},1]$  are statistically indistinguishable, we use a hybrid argument:

• Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the distribution in ExptHide[ $\mathcal{A}, \mathcal{S}, 0$ ]. Namely, the challenger begins by sampling crs  $\leftarrow$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}$ , hiding) and (pk, sk)  $\leftarrow$  KeyGen(crs). For each challenge query, the challenger first samples  $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}) \leftarrow$  GenBits(crs, pk) and gives r to  $\mathcal{A}$  to receive a set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ . It then replies with  $\sigma$  and  $\{\pi_i\}_{i \in I}$ .

•  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ , except the challenger computes  $(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_1(1^\lambda, 1^\rho)$  and uses  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}, \mathrm{and} \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}}$  in place of  $\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{pk}, \mathrm{and} \mathsf{sk}$ , respectively.

Specifically, the challenger samples  $\mathbf{V} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times k}$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_\rho \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ ,  $a \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ , and  $\mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_\rho \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ . For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , it sets  $\mathbf{z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i$ . It sets  $\widetilde{\mathbf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_\rho})$ ,  $\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_\rho})$ , and  $\widetilde{\mathsf{sk}} = (a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_\rho)$ . On each challenge query, the challenger samples  $\mathbf{y} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$  and computes  $[\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}} \mid \mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}] \leftarrow \mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{W}$ ,  $r_i \leftarrow H(g^{t_i})$ , and  $g^{u_i} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{z}_i}$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , where  $\mathbf{W}$  is defined in Eq. (C.1). The challenger sends r to the adversary and receives a set  $I \subseteq [t]$ . The challenger replies with  $\sigma = g^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}}$  and  $\{\pi_i\}_{i \in I} = \{(g^{t_i}, g^{u_i})\}_{i \in I}$ .

- $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ : Same as  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ , except when responding to the challenge queries, instead of sampling  $\mathbf{y} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$ , the challenger samples  $\mathbf{t} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho}$  and  $\mathbf{c} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ . It sets  $\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow [\mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mid \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}] \cdot \mathbf{W}^{-1}$  (and outputs  $\bot$  if  $\mathbf{W}$  is not invertible). All remaining components are constructed as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ .
- Hyb<sub>3</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>2</sub> except when responding to the challenge queries, the challenger first samples  $r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^k$ . For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , it samples  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  conditioned on  $H(g^{t_i}) = r_i$ . The challenger uses the same rejection procedure for this step as in  $\mathcal{S}_2$  to implement this. It samples  $\mathbf{c} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and the remaining components as in Hyb<sub>2</sub>.
- Hyb<sub>4</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>3</sub> except when responding to the challenge queries, the challenger samples  $\mathbf{t}$  after it receives the challenge set. In particular, on each query, after the challenger receives the set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ , it samples  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  conditioned on  $H(g^{t_i}) = r_i$  if  $i \in I$  and  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  otherwise. All remaining components are constructed as in Hyb<sub>3</sub>. This is the distribution in ExptHide[ $\mathcal{A}, \mathcal{S}, 1$ ].

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output distribution of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , the output distributions of each consecutive pair of hybrids are either statistically indistinguishable or identically distributed.

**Lemma C.4.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(A) \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_1(A)$ .

*Proof.* Since  $S_1(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$  samples  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}$ ,  $\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}$ , and  $\widetilde{\mathsf{sk}}$  using the same procedure as Setup and KeyGen, the output distributions of hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  are identically distributed.

**Lemma C.5.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(A) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_2(A)$ .

*Proof.* With overwhelming probability,  $\mathbf{W}$  is full-rank and thus invertible. In this case, the distributions of  $\mathbf{y}$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  are identical. Thus, the output distributions of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  are statistically indistinguishable.

<span id="page-43-0"></span>**Lemma C.6.** If  $\mathcal{H}$  is statistically uniform, then for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_3(\mathcal{A})$ .

Proof. Since H is sampled from a hash family that satisfies statistical uniformity, the distribution of  $H(g^{t_i})$  when  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  is statistically close to uniform over  $\{0,1\}$ . Thus, in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , each bit  $r_i$  is statistically close to uniform. It suffices to argue that the sampling algorithm in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  does not abort. In this is the case, then the two distributions are statistically close. Again, using the fact that  $\mathcal{H}$  is statistically uniform, for a random  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\Pr[H(g^{t_i}) = r_i] \geq 1/2 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  for any  $r_i \in \{0,1\}$ . Thus, with overwhelming probability, the challenger successfully finds a  $t_i$  after  $\lambda$  independent attempts. Since  $\rho = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and the adversary makes at most  $q = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  queries, the claim now follows by a union bound.

**Lemma C.7.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3(A) \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_4(A)$ .

*Proof.* First, the challenger's first message to the adversary in both experiments is  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{sk}})$  where  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_\rho})$ ,  $\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_\rho})$ , and  $\widetilde{\mathsf{sk}} = (a, \mathbf{b}_1, \dots, \mathbf{b}_\rho)$ . These components are identically distributed in the two experiments. We consider the challenge queries. On each challenge query, in both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ , the challenger sends the adversary a random string  $r \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^k$ . To conclude the proof, we argue that the challenger's response to each challenge query is identically distributed in the two experiments. In particular, after the adversary outputs a set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ , the challenger replies with a commitment  $\sigma = g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}$  and a collection of proofs  $\{\pi_i\}_{i\in I} = \{(g^{t_i}, g^{u_i})\}_{i\in I}$ . We show that  $\mathbf{c}, t_i, u_i$  for  $i \in I$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$  are identically distributed:

- In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ , for all  $i \in I$ , the challenger samples  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to  $H(g^{t_i}) = r_i$ . Thus, the distribution of  $t_i$  for each  $i \in I$  is identically distributed in the two experiments.
- In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ ,  $\mathbf{c} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  is independent and uniformly distributed.
- In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ ,  $u_i = \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}_i = \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} (\mathbf{w}_i a + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{b}_i) = t_i a + \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_i$ . Since  $a, \mathbf{b}_i, t_i, \mathbf{c}$  are all identically distributed in the two experiments for  $i \in I$ , we conclude that  $u_i$  is identically distributed in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$  for all  $i \in I$ .

<span id="page-44-0"></span>Theorem 4.8 now follows by a hybrid argument.

**Remark C.8** (Perfect Simulation in Hiding Mode). A modified version of Construction 4.3 can be shown to satisfy *perfect* simulation in hiding mode. Specifically, we make the following adjustments:

- Modify  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^\rho, \mathsf{hiding})$  so that it always outputs  $\mathbf{V}, \mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_\rho$  such that the matrix  $\mathbf{W}$  in Eq. (C.1) is full-rank. This property already holds with overwhelming probability, so this only introduces a negligible loss to the CRS indistinguishability property. With this change,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  in the proof of Theorem 4.8 are identically distributed.
- Replace the hash family  $\mathcal{H}$  with one that is *perfectly* uniform and one where there is a procedure to *exactly* sample  $t_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that  $H(g^{t_i}) = r_i$ .

Currently,  $\mathcal{H}$  is a hash family from a *prime-order* group  $\mathbb{G}$  to  $\{0,1\}$ , so perfect uniformity is impossible. However, we can modify the construction to use a hash function with domain  $\mathbb{G}\setminus\{g^0\}$ , where  $g^0\in\mathbb{G}$  denotes the identity element. To enforce this, we modify the GenBits algorithm to sample  $\mathbf{y} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+k}$  subject to the restriction that  $t_i=(\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{W})_i\neq 0$  for all  $i\in[\rho]$ . A randomly-sampled  $\mathbf{y}$ , will satisfy this property with overwhelming probability (since  $1/p=\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ ), and thus, this can only change the distribution of GenBits by a negligible amount. With this modification and assuming that  $\mathcal{H}$  is a perfectly uniform family of hash functions from  $\mathbb{G}\setminus\{[0]\}$  to  $\{0,1\}$ , and that there is an efficient algorithm to sample  $t_i\stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that  $H(g^{t_i})=r_i$ , then hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$  in the proof of Theorem 4.8 are identically distributed. As we show in Section 6, it is straightforward to construct a hash function with these properties when  $\mathbb{G}$  is an elliptic curve group over a finite field (see Remark 6.3).

With these modifications, each pair of adjacent hybrids in the proof of Theorem 4.8 is identically distributed, and we conclude that  $\Pi_{\mathsf{HBG}}$  satisfies perfect simulation in hiding mode. Combined with Construction 3.4, this gives a dual-mode NIZK with *perfect* zero-knowledge in hiding mode.

#### <span id="page-45-0"></span>C.2 Analysis of Construction 4.13 (Publicly-Verifiable HBG from Pairings)

In this section, we give the proofs for the correctness and security theorems (Theorems 4.14 to 4.17) for the publicly-verifiable hidden-bits generator from pairings (Construction 4.13).

**Proof of Theorem 4.14 (Correctness).** Fix  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , a polynomial  $\rho = \rho(\lambda)$ , and an index  $i \in [\rho]$ . Let  $\operatorname{crs} \leftarrow \operatorname{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$  and  $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}) \leftarrow \operatorname{GenBits}(\operatorname{crs})$ . Consider the value of Verify( $\operatorname{crs}, \sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i$ ). First, by construction,

$$\mathsf{crs} = \left(\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^\mathbf{V}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^\mathbf{D}, \left\{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}\right\}_{i \in [\rho]}\right)$$

 $\hat{\mathbf{a}}^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{D}, \ \mathbf{Z}_i = \mathbf{w}_i \mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}} + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{B}_i, \ \text{and} \ \hat{\mathbf{B}}_i = \mathbf{B}_i^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{D}.$  In addition,  $\sigma = g_1^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}} = g_1^{\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{V}}, \ r_i = H(g_1^{t_i}),$  and  $\pi_i = (g_1^{t_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^{\mathsf{T}}}),$  where

$$g_1^{t_i} = g_1^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{w}_i$$
 and  $g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^\mathsf{T}} = g_1^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{Z}_i = g_1^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}} (\mathbf{w}_i \mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{V} \mathbf{B}_i)$ .

It suffices to check Eq. (4.3) holds. By construction,

$$e(g_1^{t_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T}}) \cdot e(g_1^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}) = e(g_1, g_2)^{(\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{w}_i)(\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{D}) + (\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{V})(\mathbf{B}_i\mathbf{D})} = e(g_1, g_2)^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}(\mathbf{w}_i\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{V}\mathbf{B}_i)\mathbf{D}} = e(g_1^{\mathbf{u}_i^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^\mathbf{D}),$$
 and Verify accepts.

**Proof of Theorem 4.15 (Succinctness).** The length of a commitment in Construction 4.13 consists of k = O(1) elements in  $\mathbb{G}_1$ , each of which can be represented by a string of length  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ . Thus,  $|\sigma| = k \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda) = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ .

**Proof of Theorem 4.16 (Computational Binding).** We begin by constructing an efficient extractor  $\mathcal{E} = (\mathcal{E}_1, \mathcal{E}_2)$  as follows:

- $\mathcal{E}_1(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}) \to (\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}})$ : Sample  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, g_1, g_2, e) \leftarrow \mathsf{PairingGroupGen}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $H \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$ . Sample  $\mathbf{V} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k)\times k}$ . For all  $i \in [\rho]$ , sample  $\mathbf{s}_i \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and set  $\mathbf{w}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$ . Sample verification components  $\mathbf{a} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}, \mathbf{B}_1, \dots, \mathbf{B}_{\rho} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times (k+1)}, \mathbf{d} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and set  $\mathbf{D}$  as in Eq. (4.2). Then, set  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow \mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{D}$  and for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , let  $\mathbf{Z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{w}_i\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{V}\mathbf{B}_i$  and  $\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{B}_i\mathbf{D}$  as in Setup. Output  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^\mathbf{V}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\mathbf{D}}, \left\{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{b}}_i}\right\}_{i \in [\rho]})$  and  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}} = (H, \mathbf{s}_1, \dots, \mathbf{s}_k)$ .
- $\mathcal{E}_2(\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \sigma) \to r$ : On input  $\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{E}} = (H, \mathbf{s}_1, \dots, \mathbf{s}_k)$  and a commitment  $\sigma = g_1^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}}$ , compute  $r_i \leftarrow H(g_1^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{s}_i)$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$ . Output r. This is a deterministic algorithm by construction.

We now show that  $\mathcal{E}$  satisfies the two required properties.

**CRS** indistinguishability. The only difference in the CRS output by Setup and that output by  $\mathcal{E}_1$  is in how the  $\mathbf{w}_i$  are sampled (from  $\mathbf{w}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  in Setup to  $\mathbf{w}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$  where  $\mathbf{s}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  in  $\mathcal{E}_1$ ). This is precisely the distinction between the CRS in the hiding mode and the CRS in the binding mode in Construction 4.3. As such, the proof follows via the same argument as in the proof of Theorem 4.6 (by appealing to k-Lin in  $\mathbb{G}_1$ ).

**Binding.** We use a hybrid argument:

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the real binding experiment. Namely, the challenger starts by sampling  $(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$  and gives  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^{\mathbf{V}}, g_2^{\mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}}}, g_2^{\mathbf{D}}, \{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i}\}_{i \in [\rho]})$  to the adversary. Here,  $\mathbf{w}_i = \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$ ,  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{D}$ ,  $\mathbf{Z}_i = \mathbf{w}_i\mathbf{a}^{\mathsf{T}} + \mathbf{V}\mathbf{B}_i$ , and  $\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i = \mathbf{B}_i\mathbf{D}$ . The adversary then outputs a tuple  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*)$ . The output of the experiment is 1 if  $r^* \neq r_i$  where  $r \leftarrow \mathcal{E}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \sigma^*)$  and  $\mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1$ . Otherwise, the output is 0.
- Hyb<sub>1</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>0</sub> except after the adversary outputs its tuple  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*)$ , the challenger performs the following additional check. First, write  $\sigma^* = g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$  and  $\pi^* = (g_1^{t^*}, g_1^{(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}})$ . The output of Hyb<sub>1</sub> is 0 if Verify( $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*$ ) = 1 and  $t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*} \neq (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}$ . Otherwise, the output of Hyb<sub>1</sub> is computed as in Hyb<sub>0</sub>.

We write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . To complete the proof, we show that for all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , the output distributions of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  are computationally indistinguishable, and moreover, that  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  outputs 1 with negligible probability. This means that the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  (the real binding experiment) is 1 with negligible probability, which proves the claim.

**Lemma C.9.** Suppose the k-KerLin assumption holds in  $\mathbb{G}_2$  with respect to PairingGroupGen. Then, for all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A}) \overset{c}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* The only difference between  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  is the additional check the challenger performs at the end of the experiment. Suppose there exists an efficient adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  such that  $\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})=1]>\Pr[\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})=1]+\varepsilon$  for some non-negligible  $\varepsilon$ . We use  $\mathcal{A}$  to construct an adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  that breaks the k-KerLin assumption:

1. Algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  receives the k-KerLin challenge  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, g_1, g_2, e)$  and a matrix  $g_2^{\mathbf{D}}$  from the challenger (where  $\mathbf{D} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times k}$  has the form in Eq. (4.2)). It uses the challenge to simulate a CRS as follows. First, it samples  $H \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathbf{V} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho+k) \times k}$ , and vectors  $\mathbf{s}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{s}_k \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ . It also samples verification components  $\mathbf{a} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}, \mathbf{B}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{B}_\rho \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times (k+1)}$  Finally, it sets

$$\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = \left(\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^\mathbf{V}, g_2^{\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{D}}, g_2^\mathbf{D}, \left\{g_1^{\mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{V}\mathbf{B}_i}, g_2^{\mathbf{B}_i\mathbf{D}}\right\}_{i \in [\rho]}\right).$$

Note that  $\mathcal{B}$  can efficiently compute the components  $g_2^{\mathbf{a}^T\mathbf{D}}$  and  $g_2^{\mathbf{B}_i\mathbf{D}}$  from the challenge component  $g_2^{\mathbf{D}}$  since it knows  $\mathbf{a}$  and  $\mathbf{B}_i$ . The challenger gives  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

- 2. Algorithm  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs  $\sigma^* = g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^{\mathsf{T}}}, i^* \in [\rho], r^* \in \{0, 1\}, \text{ and } \pi^* = (g_1^{t^*}, g_1^{(\mathbf{u}^*)^{\mathsf{T}}}).$
- 3. Algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs the group element  $g_1^{t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T}+(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*}-(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$ . This can be computed efficiently given  $g_1^{t^*}$ ,  $g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$ ,  $g_1^{(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$ ,  $\mathbf{a}$ , and  $\mathbf{B}_{i^*}$ , all of which are known to  $\mathcal{B}$ .

By construction,  $\mathcal{B}$  perfectly simulates  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}$  according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ . Thus, with probability  $\varepsilon$ , algorithm  $\mathcal{A}$  will output  $g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}, g_1^{t^*}$ , and  $g_1^{(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$  such that  $\mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1$  and  $t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*} \neq (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}$ . If the output of  $\mathsf{Verify}$  is 1, then it must be the case that

$$e(g_1^{t^*}, g_2^{\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{D}}) \cdot e(g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\mathbf{B}_{i^*} \mathbf{D}}) = e(g_1^{(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}}, g_2^{\mathbf{D}}),$$

or equivalently,

$$t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{D} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*}\mathbf{D} = (t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*})\mathbf{D} = (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{D},$$

This means that  $(t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*} - (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T})\mathbf{D} = 0$ . Since  $t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*} \neq (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}$ , this means that  $g_1^{t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*} - (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$  is a valid solution to the k-KerLin challenge. Thus,  $\mathcal{B}$  breaks k-KerLin with the same advantage  $\varepsilon$ .

To complete the proof, it suffices to show that in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ , the output is 0 with overwhelming probability. We show that this is the case for all (possibly unbounded) adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ . Our analysis will rely on the following claim from [KW15, Lemma 2]:

<span id="page-47-0"></span>**Lemma C.10** ([KW15, Lemma 2]). Let n, t, k be integers. Fix any matrix  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n \times t}$  and  $\mathbf{D} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times k}$ . Then, for all (possibly unbounded) adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , if we sample  $\mathbf{K} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{n \times (k+1)}$  and  $(\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{y}) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathbf{M}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{K}, \mathbf{K}\mathbf{D})$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathbf{y} \notin \operatorname{span}(\mathbf{M}) \wedge \mathbf{z}^{\mathsf{T}} = \mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{K}] \le 1/p.$$

To complete the proof, let  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g_1^\mathbf{V}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{a}}}, g_2^\mathbf{D}, \left\{g_1^{\mathbf{w}_i}, g_1^{\mathbf{Z}_i}, g_2^{\hat{\mathbf{b}}_i}\right\}_{i \in [\rho]})$  be the simulated CRS in Hyb<sub>1</sub>, and let  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*)$  be the adversary's output, where  $\sigma^* = g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}}$  and  $\pi^* = (g_1^{t^*}, g_1^{(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}})$ . The state  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}$  is  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}} = (H, \mathbf{s}_1, \dots, \mathbf{s}_k)$ . In this case,  $\mathbf{w}_i = \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$ ,  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}^\mathsf{T} = \mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{D}$ ,  $\mathbf{Z}_i = \mathbf{w}_i\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + \mathbf{V}\mathbf{B}_i$ , and  $\hat{\mathbf{B}}_i = \mathbf{B}_i\mathbf{D}$ . We now show that with overwhelming probability, the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is 0:

- If  $\mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 0$ , then the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is 0 by definition.
- Suppose  $t^* = (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_{i^*}$ . Then, if  $\mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1$ , it must be the case that  $r^* = H(g_1^{t^*}) = H(g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{s}_{i^*})$ . Let  $r \leftarrow \mathcal{E}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{E}}, \sigma^*)$ . By definition  $r_i = H(g_1^{(\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{s}_{i^*})$ . But in this case,  $r^* = r_i$ , and the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is 0.
- Suppose that  $t^* \neq (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_{i^*}$ . Here, we will rely on Lemma C.10. Let  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho(\rho+k)}$  be the vector formed by stacking  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\rho}$ , and define matrices  $\mathbf{K}$  and  $\mathbf{M}$  as follows:

$$\mathbf{K} = \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} \\ \mathbf{B}_1 \\ \vdots \\ \mathbf{B}_{\rho} \end{bmatrix} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\rho k + 1) \times (k + 1)} \quad \text{and} \quad \mathbf{M}^\mathsf{T} = [\mathbf{w} \mid \mathbf{I}_{\rho} \otimes \mathbf{V}] \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho(\rho + k) \times (\rho k + 1)}.$$

By construction, observe that all of the group elements in  $\widetilde{\mathbf{crs}}$  are a function of the components of  $\mathbf{M}$  (i.e., the components  $\mathbf{V}$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_i$ ),  $\mathbf{D}$ ,  $\mathbf{M}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{K}$  (i.e.,  $\mathbf{Z}_i$ ), and  $\mathbf{K}\mathbf{D}$  (i.e.,  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}, \hat{\mathbf{B}}_i$ ). Thus, by Lemma C.10, over a uniform choice of  $\mathbf{K}$  (correspondingly, over a random choice of the verification parameters  $\mathbf{a}$ ,  $\mathbf{B}_i$ ), no adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  (given  $\mathbf{M}$ ,  $\mathbf{D}$ ,  $\mathbf{M}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{K}$ , and  $\mathbf{K}\mathbf{D}$ ) can output  $\mathbf{y}$  and  $\mathbf{u}^*$  such that  $\mathbf{y} \notin \mathrm{span}(\mathbf{M})$  and  $(\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T} = \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{K}$ , except with negligible probability. Consider  $\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} = [t^* \mid \mathbf{e}_{i^*}^\mathsf{T} \otimes (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}] \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho+1}$ , where  $\mathbf{e}_{i^*} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho}$  denotes the  $(i^*)^{\mathrm{th}}$  basis vector. Since  $t^* \neq (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{s}_{i^*}$ ,  $\mathbf{y} \notin \mathrm{span}(\mathbf{M})$ , so by Lemma C.10,  $\Pr[\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{K} = (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Since  $\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{K} = t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*}$ , this means that with overwhelming probability,

$$t^*\mathbf{a}^\mathsf{T} + (\mathbf{c}^*)^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{B}_{i^*} \neq (\mathbf{u}^*)^\mathsf{T}.$$

In this case, if  $\mathsf{Verify}(\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1$ , then the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is 0 by definition. (And if the output of  $\mathsf{Verify}$  is 0, the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  is also 0). With overwhelming probability, the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  in this case is 0.

**Proof of Theorem 4.17 (Statistical Simulation).** Follows by the same argument as in the proof of Theorem 4.8.

#### <span id="page-48-0"></span>C.3 Analysis of Construction 4.18 (Dual-Mode (Malicious) HBG from k-Lin)

In this section, we give the proofs for the correctness and security theorems (Theorems 4.19 to 4.23) for the hidden-bits generator with malicious security from the k-Lin assumption (Construction 4.18).

**Proof of Theorem 4.19 (Correctness).** Follows by an analogous argument as in the proof of Theorem 4.4.

**Proof of Theorem 4.20 (Succinctness).** The commitment  $\sigma$  in Construction 4.18 consists of a PRG seed  $s \in \{0,1\}^{\kappa}$  where  $\kappa = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and k = O(1) group elements  $g^{\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}} \in \mathbb{G}^{k}$ . Thus,  $|\sigma| = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ .

**Proof of Theorem 4.21 (CRS Indistinguishability).** Same as the proof of Theorem 4.6.

**Proof of Theorem 4.22 (Statistical Binding).** This follows by an analogous argument as the proof of Theorem 4.7. In particular, we first define the following (inefficient) Open algorithm:

• Open(crs,  $\sigma$ )  $\to r$ : On input a crs =  $(\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$  and a commitment  $\sigma = (\mathsf{s}, g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}})$ , the open algorithm first recovers  $\mathbf{V}, \mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\ell'}$  by solving the discrete logarithm problem over  $\mathbb{G}$ . For each  $i \in [\ell']$ , it checks that  $\mathbf{w}_i = \mathbf{V}\mathbf{s}_i$  for some  $\mathbf{s}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , and outputs  $\bot$  if not. If all checks pass, it computes  $(\hat{S}_1, \dots, \hat{S}_\rho, \boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(\mathsf{s})$  and the shifted sets  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$ . It computes  $r_i \leftarrow H(\prod_{j \in S_i} g^{\alpha_j \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_j})$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , and outputs r.

As in the proof of Theorem 4.7, we use a hybrid argument to complete the proof:

- $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ : This is the real binding experiment.
- Hyb<sub>1</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>0</sub>, except when generating the public key  $\mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}})$ , the challenger samples  $\mathbf{x}_1, \dots, \mathbf{x}_{\ell'} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and sets  $\mathbf{z}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{V}\mathbf{x}_i$ . Afterwards, it samples  $a \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and sets  $\mathbf{b}_i \leftarrow \mathbf{x}_i \mathbf{s}_i a$  for use as the (secret) verification coefficients.
- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub> except the challenger implements Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma$ , i,  $r_i$ ,  $\pi_i$ ) by computing Verify\* $(\sigma, r_i, \pi_i, (\mathbf{s}_1, \dots, \mathbf{s}_{\ell'}), (\mathbf{x}_1, \dots, \mathbf{x}_{\ell'}))$ , which does the following. On input  $\sigma = (\mathbf{s}, g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}})$ ,  $r_i \in \{0, 1\}, \ \pi_i = \{(j, g^{t_j}, g^{u_j})\}_{j \in S}$  for some implicitly-defined set  $S \subseteq [\ell']$ , and vectors  $\mathbf{s}_1, \dots, \mathbf{s}_{\ell'} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ ,  $\mathbf{x}_1, \dots, \mathbf{x}_{\ell'} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ , compute  $(\hat{S}_1, \dots, \hat{S}_\rho, \alpha) \leftarrow G(\mathbf{s})$ , and the shifted set  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$ . It then checks the following conditions and outputs 1 only of them are satisfied (and outputs 0 otherwise):

$$S = S_i$$
 and  $r_i = H(\prod_{j \in S_i} g^{\alpha_j t_j})$  and  $\forall j \in S_i : t_j = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_j$  and  $\mathbf{u}_j = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}_j$ 

As usual, we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . Hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(\mathcal{A})$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  are identical by construction. Hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A})$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$  are statistically indistinguishable by a similar argument as that in the proof of Lemma C.3. Namely, the Verify algorithm in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  outputs 1 only if for all  $j \in S_i$ :

$$u_j = t_j a + \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{b}_j = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} (\mathbf{s}_j a + \mathbf{b}_j) + (t_j - \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_j) a = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{x}_j + (t_j - \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_j) a.$$

If there is some  $j \in S_i$  where  $t_j \neq \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_j$ , then we can argue (as in the proof of Lemma C.3) that with overwhelming probability over the choice of a, the verifier in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  outputs 0. Conversely, if  $t_j = \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{s}_j$  for all  $j \in S_i$ , then  $\mathsf{Verify}$  and  $\mathsf{Verify}^*$  behave identically, and the claim follows. Finally, by a similar argument as in the proof of Theorem 4.7, the output of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is always 0.

**Proof of Theorem 4.23 (Statistical Simulation).** We construct a simulator  $S = (S_1, S_2)$  as follows:

•  $S_1(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}) \to (\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}}, \widetilde{\operatorname{crs}})$ : Sample  $\mathcal{G} = (\mathbb{G}, p, g) \leftarrow \operatorname{GroupGen}(1^{\lambda}), H \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}, \mathbf{V} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k) \times k},$  $\mathbf{w}_1, \dots, \mathbf{w}_{\ell'} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'+k}$  exactly as in  $\operatorname{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \operatorname{hiding})$ . In the following, we will write

$$\mathbf{W} = [\ \mathbf{w}_1 \ | \ \cdots \ | \ \mathbf{w}_{\ell'} \ | \ \mathbf{V} \ ] \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k)\times(\ell'+k)}.$$

Output  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G}, H, g^{\mathbf{V}}, g^{\mathbf{w}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$ , and  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}} = (\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}, \mathbf{W})$ 

- $S_2(\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}},\operatorname{pk},I,r_I) \to (\widetilde{\sigma},\{\widetilde{\pi}_i\}_{i\in I})$ : On input the simulation state  $\operatorname{st}_{\mathcal{S}}=(\widetilde{\operatorname{crs}},\mathbf{W})$  where  $\widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}=(\mathcal{G},H,g^{\mathbf{V}},g^{\mathbf{w}_1},\ldots,g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$ , a public key  $\operatorname{pk}=(g^{\mathbf{z}_1},\ldots,g^{\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}})$ , a set of indices  $I\subseteq\{0,1\}^k$ , and a bitstring  $r_I\in\{0,1\}^{|I|}$ , the simulator samples a seed  $\operatorname{\mathfrak{s}} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow}\{0,1\}^{\kappa}$  and computes  $(\hat{S}_1,\ldots,\hat{S}_\rho,\boldsymbol{\alpha})\leftarrow G(\operatorname{\mathfrak{s}})$ . For each  $i\in I$ , it computes the shifted sets  $S_i\leftarrow\{j+\ell\cdot(i-1)\mid j\in\hat{S}_i\}$ . Then, it samples a vector  $\operatorname{\mathbf{t}}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'}$  as follows:
  - For each  $i \in I$ , first sample  $t_i' \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to  $H(g^{t_i'}) = r_i$ . Specifically, the simulator repeatedly samples  $t_i' \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  until finding one that satisfies  $H(g^{t_i'}) = r_i$ . If no such  $t_i'$  is found after  $\lambda$  iterations, then the simulator aborts and outputs  $\perp$ . Then for  $j \in S_i$ , sample  $t_j \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to the constraint that  $\sum_{j \in S_i} \alpha_j t_j = t_i'$ .
  - For all of the remaining indices  $j \in [\ell'] \setminus \bigcup_{i \in I} S_i$ , sample  $t_j \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ .

Next, it samples  $\mathbf{c} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$  and computes  $\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow [\mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mid \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}] \cdot \mathbf{W}^{-1}$  (outputting  $\bot$  if  $\mathbf{W}$  is not invertible). It computes  $g^{u_j} \leftarrow g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{z}_j}$  for each  $j \in S_i$  and  $i \in I$ . Finally, for each  $i \in I$ , it sets  $\widetilde{\pi}_i = \{(j, g^{t_j}, g^{u_j})\}_{j \in S_i}$ . Finally, it outputs  $\widetilde{\sigma} = (\mathsf{s}, g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}})$  and  $\{\widetilde{\pi}_i\}_{i \in I}$ .

To show that  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{A},0]$  and  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{A},1]$  are statistically indistinguishable, we use a hybrid argument:

- Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the distribution in ExptHide[ $\mathcal{A}, \mathcal{S}, 0$ ]. Namely, the challenger begins by sampling crs  $\leftarrow$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}$ , hiding). It gives crs to  $\mathcal{A}$  to receive a public key pk. For each challenge query, the challenger samples  $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}) \leftarrow \mathsf{GenBits}(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{pk})$  and gives r to  $\mathcal{A}$  to receive a set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ . It then replies with  $\sigma$  and  $\{\pi_i\}_{i \in I}$ .
- Hyb<sub>1</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>0</sub> except the challenger computes  $(\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{S}},\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}_1(1^\lambda,1^\rho)$  and uses  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}$  in place of  $\mathsf{crs}$ . Everything else proceeds identically to  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$ . Specifically, in this experiment, the challenger samples  $\mathcal{G}$ , H, and  $\mathbf{V},\mathbf{w}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}$  as specified by  $\mathcal{S}_1$  and sets  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}} = (\mathcal{G},H,g^\mathbf{V},g^{\mathbf{w}_1},\ldots,g^{\mathbf{w}_{\ell'}})$ . It gives  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}$  to the adversary and receives a public key  $\mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1},\ldots,g^{\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}})$ . Let  $\mathbf{Z} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k)\times\ell'}$  be the matrix whose columns are  $\mathbf{z}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}$ . On a challenge query, the challenger proceeds as follows:
  - 1. Sample  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\kappa}$  and compute  $(\hat{S}_1,\ldots,\hat{S}_{\rho},\boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(s)$ . For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , compute  $S_i \leftarrow \{j+\ell\cdot(i-1) \mid j\in\hat{S}_i\}$ .

- 2. Sample  $\mathbf{y} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'+k}$  and compute  $[\mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mid \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}] \leftarrow \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{W}, \mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{Z}$ .
- 3. For each  $i \in [\rho]$ , compute  $t_i' \leftarrow \prod_{j \in S_i} g^{\alpha_j t_j}$ ,  $r_i \leftarrow H(t_i')$  and  $\pi_i \leftarrow \{(j, g^{t_j}, g^{u_j})\}_{j \in S_i}$ .
- 4. The challenger gives  $r \in \{0,1\}^{\rho}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  and receives a set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ .
- 5. The challenger replies with  $\sigma = (\mathsf{s}, g^{t_{\ell'+1}}) = (\mathsf{s}, g^{\mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}})$  and the set  $\{\pi_i\}_{i \in I}$ .
- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub>, except when responding to the challenge queries, instead of sampling  $\mathbf{y} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'+k}$ , the challenger instead samples  $\mathbf{t} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'}$  and  $\mathbf{c} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^k$ . It sets  $\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \leftarrow [\mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mid \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}] \cdot \mathbf{W}^{-1}$  (and outputs  $\bot$  if  $\mathbf{W}$  is not invertible). All remaining components are constructed as in Hyb<sub>1</sub>.
- Hyb<sub>3</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>2</sub>, except when responding to the challenge queries, the challenger first samples  $\mathbf{t}' \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho}$ . Next, for each  $i \in [\rho]$  and  $j \in S_i$ , it samples  $t_j \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to the constraint that  $\sum_{j \in S_i} \alpha_j t_j = t_i'$ . For indices  $j \in [\ell'] \setminus \bigcup_{i \in [\rho]} S_i$ , sample  $t_j \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ .
- Hyb<sub>4</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>3</sub>, except when responding to the challenge queries, the challenger first samples  $r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\rho}$ . Then, for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , it samples  $t'_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  conditioned on  $H(t'_i) = r_i$ . All remaining components are constructed as in Hyb<sub>3</sub>.
- Hyb<sub>5</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>4</sub> except when responding to the challenge queries, the challenger samples  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'}$  after it receives the challenge set. In particular, on each query, after the challenger receives the set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ , it samples  $\mathbf{t}$  as follows:
  - For each  $i \in I$ , first sample  $t_i' \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to  $H(g^{t_i'}) = r_i$ . Then, for  $j \in S_i$ , sample  $t_j \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to the constraint that  $\sum_{j \in S_i} \alpha_j t_j = t_i'$ .
  - For all of the remaining indices  $j \in [\ell' + 1] \setminus \bigcup_{i \in I} S_i$ , sample  $t_j \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ .

After sampling  $\mathbf{t}$  using the above procedure, the simulator constructs the remaining components as in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ . This is the distribution in  $\mathsf{ExptHide}[\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S},1]$ .

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output of hybrid  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . In the following, we will show that the output distribution of each pair of adjacent experiments is statistically indistinguishable (or identically distributed). Our analysis will rely on the following statistical property on the output of a secure PRG:

<span id="page-50-0"></span>Claim C.11. Suppose G is a secure PRG, and take  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\kappa}$ . Let  $(\hat{S}_1,\ldots,\hat{S}_{\rho},\boldsymbol{\alpha}) \leftarrow G(s)$ . Then, with overwhelming probability over the choice of s, the following properties hold:

- For any  $i \in [\rho \ell]$ ,  $\Pr[\alpha_i = 0] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .
- Let  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times \ell'}$  be any fixed matrix and let  $I \subseteq [\rho]$  be any fixed set of indices. For each  $i \in I$ , let  $S_i \leftarrow \{j + \ell \cdot (i-1) \mid j \in \hat{S}_i\}$  be the shifted set of indices, and define

$$J = \{j \in S_i \text{ for some } i \in I \mid j \in [\ell']\} \subseteq [\ell'].$$

Let  $\hat{\mathbf{A}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times \lambda |I|}$  be the submatrix of  $\mathbf{A}$  formed by taking only the columns of  $\mathbf{A}$  indexed by J. For  $i \in [\rho]$ , define  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell}$  as follows:

$$\alpha_j^{(i)} = \begin{cases} \alpha_{j+\ell \cdot (i-1)} & \text{if } j \in \hat{S}_i \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

By construction,  $\alpha^{(i)}$  contains exactly  $\lambda$  non-zero entries (as specified by the set  $\hat{S}_i$ ). Then, for any  $i \in [\rho] \setminus I$ ,

$$\Pr[\boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} \in \operatorname{span}(\hat{\mathbf{A}})] = \Pr[\exists \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\lambda|I|} : \boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} = \hat{\mathbf{A}}\mathbf{v}] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

*Proof.* Both properties above are efficiently-checkable given  $(\hat{S}_1, \ldots, \hat{S}_{\rho}, \boldsymbol{\alpha})$ , the matrix  $\mathbf{A}$ , and the set of indices I. It suffices to show that both hold with overwhelming probability when  $(\hat{S}_1, \ldots, \hat{S}_{\rho}, \boldsymbol{\alpha}) \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}^{\rho} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho\ell}$ . The claim then follows by PRG security. We show each property below:

- Since  $\alpha \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho\ell}$ ,  $\alpha_i = 0$  with probability  $1/p = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .
- Let  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$  be the matrix defined above, and let  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}_j^\mathsf{T} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\lambda|I|}$  denote the  $j^{\text{th}}$  row of  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$ . Let  $n = \text{rank}(\hat{\mathbf{A}}) \leq \lambda |I| \leq \lambda \rho = \ell/3$ . This means that there exists a collection of indices  $j_1, \ldots, j_n \in [\ell]$  such that the collection  $\{\hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j_1}^\mathsf{T}, \ldots, \hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j_n}^\mathsf{T}\}$  is linearly independent and  $\text{span}(\hat{\mathbf{A}}^\mathsf{T}) = \text{span}(\{\hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j_1}^\mathsf{T}, \ldots, \hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j_n}^\mathsf{T}\})$ . By construction,  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$  only depends on  $\mathbf{A}$  and the sets  $\hat{S}_i$  where  $i \in I$ . This means that we can sample the sets  $\hat{S}_i$  where  $i \notin I$  after fixing  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$  and the set of indices  $\{j_1, \ldots, j_n\}$ . In this case, since  $\hat{S}_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell}$ ,

$$\Pr[\hat{S}_i \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{T}_{\lambda,\ell} : \hat{S}_i \subseteq \{j_1, \dots, j_n\}] \le \frac{\binom{n}{\lambda}}{\binom{\ell}{\lambda}} \le \left(\frac{ne}{\ell}\right)^{\lambda} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

since  $n \leq \ell/3$ . Thus, for any  $i \in [\rho] \setminus I$ , there exists an index  $j^* \in \hat{S}_i$  where  $j^* \notin \{j_1, \ldots, j_n\}$  with overwhelming probability. In addition, since  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j^*}^\mathsf{T} \in \mathrm{span}(\hat{\mathbf{A}}^\mathsf{T})$ , there exist scalars  $\beta_1, \ldots, \beta_n \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that  $\hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j^*}^\mathsf{T} = \sum_{\tau \in [n]} \beta_\tau \hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j_\tau}^\mathsf{T}$ . This means that if there exists  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\lambda|I|}$  such that  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} = \hat{\mathbf{A}}\mathbf{v}$ , then

$$\alpha_{j^*}^{(i)} = \hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j^*}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} = \sum_{\tau \in [n]} \beta_\tau \hat{\mathbf{a}}_{j_\tau}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} = \sum_{\tau \in [n]} \beta_\tau \alpha_{j_\tau}^{(i)}.$$

Since  $j^* \in \hat{S}_i$ , by construction,  $\alpha_{j^*}^{(i)} = \alpha_{j^* + \ell \cdot (i-1)}$ , which is uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  and independent of  $\beta_{\tau}$  and  $\alpha_{j_{\tau}}^{(i)}$  for all  $\tau \in [n]$ . Thus, over the randomness of  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}$ , for any  $i \in [\rho] \setminus I$ 

$$\Pr[\exists \mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\lambda|I|} : \boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} = \hat{\mathbf{A}}\mathbf{v}] = \Pr\left[\alpha_{j^*}^{(i)} = \sum_{\tau \in [n]} \beta_\tau \alpha_{j_\tau}^{(i)}\right] = \frac{1}{p} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \quad \Box$$

<span id="page-51-0"></span>We will also use the following simple fact on linear independence.

Claim C.12. Take any matrix  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{m \times n}$  and any vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^m$  where  $\mathbf{v} \notin \operatorname{span}(\mathbf{A})$ . Then, for any  $\gamma \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , the following distributions are identical:

$$\{\mathbf{t} \overset{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^m : (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{v}, \gamma, \mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A})\} \equiv \{\mathbf{t} \overset{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \{\mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^m \mid \mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v} = \gamma\} : (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{v}, \gamma, \mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{A})\}$$

*Proof.* Follows immediately from the fact that  $\mathbf{v}$  is not in the span of  $\mathbf{A}$ .

**Lemma C.13.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(A) \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_1(A)$ .

*Proof.* Since  $S_1(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho})$  samples  $\widetilde{\mathsf{crs}}$  using the same procedure as  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \mathsf{hiding})$ , the output distributions of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  are identically distributed.

**Lemma C.14.** For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* With overwhelming probability, **W** is full-rank and invertible. In this case, the distribution of  $\mathbf{y}$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  is identical.

**Lemma C.15.** If G is a secure PRG, then for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_3(\mathcal{A})$ .

Proof. These two distributions are identical provided that for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , there is at least one index  $j \in S_i$  where  $\alpha_j \neq 0$ . In this case, both  $\mathbf{t}$  and  $\mathbf{t}'$  are uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'}$  and  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\rho}$ , respectively, subject to the condition that  $t'_i = \sum_{j \in S_i} \alpha_j t_j$  for all  $i \in [\rho]$ . By Claim C.11, with overwhelming probability,  $\alpha_j \neq 0$  for any  $j \in [\ell']$ . Since  $\rho = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , the claim follows by a union bound.

**Lemma C.16.** If  $\mathcal{H}$  is statistically uniform, then for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_4(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_3$ , the challenger samples  $t_i' \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and sets  $r_i \leftarrow H(t_i')$  while, in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ , the challenger samples  $r_i \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}$  and sets  $t_i' \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to  $H(t_i') = r_i$ . These two distributions are statistically indistinguishable by the argument from the proof of Lemma C.6.

**Lemma C.17.** If G is a secure PRG, then for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_5(\mathcal{A})$ .

Proof. The challenger samples  $\widetilde{\operatorname{crs}}$  identically in the two experiments, so it suffices to consider its responses to the challenge queries. Let  $\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k)\times(\ell'+k)}$  be the matrix of components in the CRS and let  $\mathsf{pk} = (g^{\mathbf{z}_1}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{z}_{\ell'}})$  be the adversary's chosen public key. As above, let  $\mathbf{Z} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k)\times\ell'}$  be the matrix whose columns are  $\mathbf{z}_1, \dots, \mathbf{z}_{\ell'}$ . Now, on each challenge query, in both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_5$ , the challenger starts by sending the adversary a random string  $r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\rho}$ , and the adversary replies with a set  $I \subseteq [\rho]$ . The challenger then replies with a commitment  $\sigma = (\mathsf{s}, g^{\mathsf{c}^\mathsf{T}})$  and a set of proofs  $\{\pi_i\}_{i\in I}$ , where  $\pi_i = \{(j, g^{t_j}, g^{u_j})\}_{j\in S_i}$ . To complete the proof, it suffices to show that the challenger's response is statistically indistinguishable in the two experiments. To facilitate the analysis, we first define the following variables:

- For  $i \in [\rho]$ , let  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell}$  be the vector where  $t_j^{(i)} = t_{j+\ell \cdot (i-1)}$ . In other words,  $\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}} = [(\mathbf{t}^{(1)})^{\mathsf{T}} \mid \cdots \mid (\mathbf{t}^{(\rho)})^{\mathsf{T}}] \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell'}$ .
- For  $i \in [\rho]$ , define  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell}$  as follows:

$$\alpha_j^{(i)} = \begin{cases} \alpha_{j+\ell \cdot (i-1)} & \text{if } j \in \hat{S}_i \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

By construction, in both experiments,  $t'_i = \sum_{j \in [\ell]} \alpha_j^{(i)} t_j^{(i)}$ .

We now consider the different components in the two experiments:

- The string r is independently and uniformly sampled from  $\{0,1\}^{\rho}$  in both experiments.
- The seed s is independently and uniformly sampled from  $\{0,1\}^{\kappa}$  in both experiments, so the seed s, the sets  $S_1, \ldots, S_{\rho}$ , and the vector  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}$  are identically distributed in the two experiments.

- The commitment vector  $\mathbf{c}$  is independently and uniformly sampled from  $\mathbb{Z}_p^k$  in both experiments.
- For  $i \in I$ ,  $t'_i$  is sampled identically in the two experiments (namely,  $t'_i \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  subject to  $H(g^{t'_i}) = r_i$ ). Correspondingly, the vectors  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  for all  $i \in I$  are identically distributed.

It suffices to show that in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_5$ , the components  $\{u_j\}_{j\in S_i}$  for all  $i\in I$  are statistically indistinguishable (given all of the other components in the adversary's view). In both experiments,

$$\mathbf{u}^\mathsf{T} = \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{Z} = [\ \mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \ | \ \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T} \ ] \cdot \mathbf{W}^{-1} \mathbf{Z}.$$

Let

$$\mathbf{A} = \mathbf{W}^{-1}\mathbf{Z} = \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathbf{A}^{(1)} \\ \vdots \\ \mathbf{A}^{(\rho)} \\ \mathbf{A}' \end{array} \right] \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k) \times \ell'},$$

where  $\mathbf{A}^{(i)} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times \ell'}$  and  $\mathbf{A}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times \ell'}$ . This is a *fixed* matrix (determined by the CRS and the adversary's choice of public key). Moreover, the adversary's choice of indices  $i \in I$  is *fixed* before the challenger samples the commitment and the openings. Let

$$J = \{j \in S_i \text{ for some } i \in I \mid j \in [\ell']\}$$

be the set of indices that appear in some set  $S_i$  for  $i \in I$ . Let  $\hat{\mathbf{A}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell'+k)\times \lambda|I|}$  be the submatrix of  $\mathbf{A}$  formed by taking only the columns of  $\mathbf{A}$  indexed by the set J. In particular, the elements of  $[\mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \mid \mathbf{c}^\mathsf{T}] \cdot \hat{\mathbf{A}}$  precisely coincide with  $\bigcup_{i \in I} \{u_j\}_{j \in S_i}$ , which are the elements in the adversary's view. Now, write  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$  as follows:

<span id="page-53-0"></span>
$$\hat{\mathbf{A}} = \left[ \begin{array}{c} \hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(1)} \ \vdots \ \hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(\rho)} \ \hat{\mathbf{A}}^{\prime} \end{array} \right],$$

where  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(i)} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times \lambda|I|}$  and  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times \lambda|I|}$ . With this, we can write

$$[\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}} \mid \mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}] \cdot \hat{\mathbf{A}} = \sum_{i \in [\rho]} \left( (\mathbf{t}^{(i)})^{\mathsf{T}} \hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(i)} \right) + \mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}} \hat{\mathbf{A}}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\lambda |I|}$$
(C.2)

Since  $\mathbf{c}$  and all of the  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  are sampled independently, we can consider each summand individually:

- If  $i \in I$ , then as argued above,  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  is identically distributed in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_5$ . Since  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$  is a fixed matrix (independent of  $\mathbf{t}$ ), the products  $(\mathbf{t}^{(i)})^\mathsf{T}\hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(i)}$  are also identically distributed.
- If  $i \notin I$ , then the  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  in the two experiments are sampled from different distributions. In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$ ,  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  is uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$  subject to  $\sum_{j \in [\ell]} \alpha_j^{(i)} t_j^{(i)} = t_i'$  while, in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_5$ ,  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  is uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$ .

Since  $i \notin I$  and  $\mathbf{A}^{(i)} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times \ell'}$  is a fixed matrix, we can appeal to Claim C.11 to conclude that the probability that  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} \in \operatorname{span}(\hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(i)})$  is negligible. By Claim C.12, this means that with overwhelming probability, the distribution of  $(\mathbf{t}^{(i)})^{\mathsf{T}} \hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(i)}$  is statistically indistinguishable in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_4$  (where  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  is uniform subject to a linear constraint  $\boldsymbol{\alpha}^{(i)} \notin \operatorname{span}(\hat{\mathbf{A}}^{(i)})$ ) and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_5$  (where  $\mathbf{t}^{(i)}$  is uniform).

• As argued above,  $\mathbf{c}$  is identically distributed in the two experiments (and independent of  $\hat{\mathbf{A}}$ ). Thus,  $\mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}\hat{\mathbf{A}}'$  is identically distributed in the two experiments.

Since every term in Eq. (C.2) is either statistically indistinguishable or identically distributed in the two experiments, we conclude that  $[\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}} \mid \mathbf{c}^{\mathsf{T}}] \cdot \hat{\mathbf{A}}$  is also statistically indistinguishable. Based on the above analysis, the adversary's view in each challenge query is statistically indistinguishable in Hyb<sub>4</sub> and Hyb<sub>5</sub>. Since the adversary makes at most a polynomial number of challenge queries, the claim follows by a hybrid argument.

Since each consecutive pair of hybrid experiments is statistically indistinguishable (or identically distributed), the claim follows.  $\Box$ 

# D Analysis of Constructions from QR (Section 5)

In this section, we provide the analysis of the dual-mode hidden-bits generators from Section 5.

## <span id="page-54-0"></span>D.1 Analysis of Construction 5.3 (Dual-Mode HBG from QR)

In this section, we give the proofs for the correctness and security theorems (Theorems 5.4 to 5.8) for the dual-mode hidden-bits generator from QR (Construction 5.3).

**Proof of Theorem 5.4 (Correctness).** Fix  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , a polynomial  $\rho = \rho(\lambda)$ , an index  $i \in [\rho]$ , and a mode mode  $\in$  {binding, hiding}. Let  $\operatorname{crs} \leftarrow \operatorname{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\rho}, \operatorname{mode})$ ,  $(\operatorname{pk}, \operatorname{sk}) \leftarrow \operatorname{KeyGen}(\operatorname{crs})$ , and  $(\sigma, r, \{\pi_i\}_{i \in [\rho]}) \leftarrow \operatorname{GenBits}(\operatorname{crs}, \operatorname{pk})$ . By construction,  $\operatorname{crs} = (N, g, h, H, g^{\mathbf{v}}, g^{s_1\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_1}, \dots, g^{s_{\rho}\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\rho}})$ , for some vectors  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}^{\rho}$  and  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}_1, \dots, \hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\rho} \in \mathbb{Z}_2^{\rho}$ . Then  $\operatorname{sk} = \{a_{\tau}, b_{\tau,i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]}$  and

$$\mathsf{pk} = \{\overline{\mathbf{z}}_{\tau,i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]} = \{(g^{s_i\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i})^{a_\tau}(g^{\mathbf{v}})^{b_{\tau,i}}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]} = \{g^{(a_\tau s_i + b_{\tau,i})\mathbf{v}}h^{a_\tau \hat{\mathbf{w}}_i}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]}.$$

Next, we have that  $\sigma = \overline{c} = \prod_{j \in [\rho]} g^{v_j y_j} = g^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v}}, r_i = \mathsf{LEQ}(\overline{t}_i, \overline{t}_i h)$  and  $\pi_i = (\overline{t}_i, u_i)$ , where

$$\bar{t}_i = \prod_{j \in [\rho]} (g^{s_i v_j} h^{\hat{w}_{i,j}})^{y_j} = g^{s_i \mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \mathbf{v}} h^{\mathbf{y}^\mathsf{T} \hat{\mathbf{w}}_i},$$

and  $u_i = H(\overline{u}_{1,i}, \dots, \overline{u}_{T,i})$  where

$$\overline{u}_{\tau,i} = \prod_{j \in [\rho]} \left( g^{(a_{\tau}s_i + b_{\tau,i}v_j)} h^{a_{\tau}\hat{w}_{i,j}} \right)^{y_j} = g^{(a_{\tau}s_i + b_{\tau,i})\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v}} h^{a_{\tau}\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\hat{\mathbf{w}}_i}$$

for all  $\tau \in [T]$ . Consider now the behavior of Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma$ , i,  $r_i$ ,  $\pi_i$ ). By construction,  $r_i = \mathsf{LEQ}(\bar{t}_i, \bar{t}_i h)$ , so it suffices to check that Eq. (5.1) holds. By construction, for each  $\tau \in [T]$ 

$$\overline{t}_{\tau}^{a_{\tau}} \overline{c}^{b_{\tau,i}} = (g^{a_{\tau}s_{i}\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v}} h^{a_{\tau}\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}} \hat{\mathbf{w}}_{i})(g^{b_{\tau,i}\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v}}) = g^{(a_{\tau}s_{i} + b_{\tau,i})\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v}} h^{a_{\tau}\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}} \hat{\mathbf{w}}_{i} = \overline{u}_{\tau,i}.$$

Thus, Eq. (5.1) is equivalent to checking whether  $u_i = H(\overline{u}_{1,i}, \dots, \overline{u}_{T,i})$ , which holds by construction, and Verify outputs 1.

**Proof of Theorem 5.5 (Succinctness).** The size of the commitment in Construction 5.3 consists of a single element in  $\mathbb{Z}_N^*$ , which has length  $\lceil \log N \rceil = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ .

**Proof of Theorem 5.6 (CRS Indistinguishability).** The proof of Theorem 5.6 follows via the following claim from [BG10]:

<span id="page-55-0"></span>Claim D.1 ([BG10, §5]). Suppose a safe prime product modulus sampler SampleModulus satisfies the QR assumption. Let h=-1. Then, for all polynomials  $\rho=\rho(\lambda)$ , all fixed vectors  $\hat{\mathbf{w}} \in \{0,1\}^{\rho}$ , and all efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , if we sample  $(N,p,q) \leftarrow \mathsf{SampleModulus}(1^{\lambda})$ ,  $\mathbf{v} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}^{\rho}$ ,  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}^{\rho}$ , where p=2p'+1 and q=2q'+1, we have that

$$\left|\Pr[\mathcal{A}(N,g,g^{\mathbf{v}},g^{s\mathbf{v}})=1] - \Pr[\mathcal{A}(N,g,g^{\mathbf{v}},g^{s\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}})=1]\right| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where g is a generator of  $\mathbb{QR}_N$ .

First, Claim D.1 also holds if we instead sample  $\mathbf{v} \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}^{\rho}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$  and  $s \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$  since the statistical distance between  $\{r \stackrel{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor} : r \bmod p'q'\}$  and  $\mathsf{Uniform}(\mathbb{Z}_{p'q'})$  is

$$\Delta(\{r \overset{\mathrm{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor} : r \bmod p'q'\}, \mathsf{Uniform}(\mathbb{Z}_{p'q'})) = \frac{(N-1)/2 \bmod p'q'}{(N-1)/2} = \frac{p'+q'}{2p'q'+p'+q'} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

since  $1/p', 1/q' = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . To complete the proof, we use a hybrid argument, where for  $j \in \{0, \dots, \rho\}$ , Hyb<sub>j</sub> is defined as follows:

•  $\mathsf{Hyb}_j$ : Sample  $(N,p,q) \leftarrow \mathsf{SampleModulus}(1^\lambda)$ ,  $\mathbf{v} \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}^\rho_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ ,  $s_1,\ldots,s_\rho \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ ,  $H \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$ , and let g be a generator of  $\mathbb{QR}_N$ . Output  $\mathsf{crs} = (N,g,h,H,g^\mathbf{v},g^{s_1\mathbf{v}},\ldots,g^{s_i\mathbf{v}},g^{s_{i+1}\mathbf{v}}h^{\mathbf{e}_{i+1}},\ldots,g^{s_\rho\mathbf{v}}h^{\mathbf{e}_\rho})$ .

By construction,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  implements  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^\rho, \mathsf{hiding})$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_\rho$  implements  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^\rho, \mathsf{binding})$ . We can appeal to Claim D.1 (where  $\mathbf{v}$  and s are sampled from  $\mathbb{Z}^\rho_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$  and  $\mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ , respectively) to argue that each pair of adjacent hybrids  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{j-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_j$  for  $j \in [\rho]$  are computationally indistinguishable. To see this, set  $\hat{\mathbf{w}} = \mathbf{e}_j$  in Claim D.1, and let  $(N, g, g^{\mathbf{v}}, g^{\mathbf{s}\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}'})$  be the challenge. We simulate a CRS by sampling  $s_1, \ldots, s_{j-1}, s_{j+1}, \ldots, s_\rho \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ ,  $H \overset{\mathbb{R}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{H}$  and outputting

$$(N,g,h,H,g^{\mathbf{v}},g^{s_1\mathbf{v}},\ldots,g^{s_{j-1}\mathbf{v}},g^{s\mathbf{v}}h^{\hat{\mathbf{w}}'},g^{s_{j+1}\mathbf{v}}h^{\mathbf{e}_{j+1}},\ldots,g^{s_{\rho}\mathbf{v}}h^{\mathbf{e}_{\rho}}).$$

If  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}' = \mathbf{0}$ , then this is precisely the distribution  $\mathsf{Hyb}_j$  and if  $\hat{\mathbf{w}}' = \mathbf{e}_j$ , then this is the distribution  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{j-1}$ . Thus, by Claim D.1, the outputs of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{j-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_j$  are computationally indistinguishable for all  $j \in [\rho]$ . Since  $\rho = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , Theorem 5.6 follows by a hybrid argument.

**Proof of Theorem 5.7 (Statistical Binding).** Recall that in binding mode, the common reference string is given by

$$\mathrm{crs} = (N, g, h, H, g^{\mathbf{v}}, g^{s_1\mathbf{v}}, \dots, g^{s_\rho\mathbf{v}}).$$

We define the (inefficient) Open algorithm as follows:

• Open(crs,  $\sigma$ )  $\to r$ : On input a crs =  $(N, g, h, H, g^{\mathbf{v}}, g^{s_1 \mathbf{v}}, \dots, g^{s_{\rho} \mathbf{v}})$  and a commitment  $\sigma = g^c$  (outputting  $\bot$  if the components do not have this form), the open algorithm recovers  $\mathbf{v}$ ,  $s_1, \dots, s_{\rho}$ , and c. Then, it computes  $r_i \leftarrow \mathsf{LEQ}(g^{cs_i}, g^{cs_i}h)$  for each  $i \in [\rho]$ , and outputs r.

To complete the proof, we use a hybrid argument:

• Hyb<sub>0</sub>: This is the real soundness experiment. The challenger samples  $\operatorname{crs} \leftarrow \operatorname{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^\lambda, 1^\rho, \operatorname{\mathsf{binding}})$  and  $(\operatorname{\mathsf{pk}}, \operatorname{\mathsf{sk}}) \leftarrow \operatorname{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}})$  and gives  $(\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}}, \operatorname{\mathsf{pk}})$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ . Here,

$$\operatorname{crs} = (N, g, h, H, g^{\mathbf{v}}, g^{s_1 \mathbf{v}}, \dots, g^{s_{\rho} \mathbf{v}}) \quad \text{and} \quad \operatorname{pk} = \{g^{(a_{\tau} s_i + b_{\tau, i}) \mathbf{v}}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]}.$$

The adversary can make queries to the verification oracle, and on each query  $(\sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i)$ , the challenger replies with  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{sk}, \sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i)$ . At the end of the game, the adversary outputs a tuple  $(\sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*)$  and the output of the experiment is 1 if  $r^* \neq r_i$  where  $r \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{crs}, \sigma^*)$  and  $\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{sk}, \sigma^*, i^*, r^*, \pi^*) = 1$ .

- Hyb<sub>1</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>0</sub> except the challenger samples the scalars  $s_1, \ldots, s_\rho$  and the secret key components  $a_\tau, b_{\tau,i}$  uniformly at random from  $\mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'}$  (instead of  $\mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}$ ) for all  $\tau \in [T]$  and  $i \in [\rho]$ .
- Hyb<sub>2</sub>: Same as Hyb<sub>1</sub> except the challenger computes Verify(crs, sk,  $\sigma$ , i,  $r_i$ ,  $\pi_i$ ) using the following modified procedure. First, parse  $\sigma = g^c h^{\hat{c}}$  and  $\pi_i = (g^{t_i} h^{\hat{t}_i}, u_i)$ , for some  $c, t_i \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}$ ,  $\hat{c}, \hat{t}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_2$ , and  $u_i \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ . Then, the challenger does the following:
  - If  $r_i \neq \mathsf{LEQ}(g^{t_i}h^{\hat{t}_i}, g^{t_i}h^{\hat{t}_i+1})$ , output 0.
  - If  $\hat{c} \neq 0$  or  $\hat{t}_i \neq 0$ , then output 0.
  - If  $t_i \neq s_i c$ , then output 0.
  - Otherwise, take any  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}^{\rho}$  such that  $\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{v} = c$ . Write the public key as  $\mathsf{pk} = \{g^{\mathbf{z}_{\tau,i}}\}_{\tau \in [T], i \in [\rho]}$  and output 1 if  $u_i = H(g^{\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{z}_{1,i}}, \dots, g^{\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{z}_{T,i}})$ . Otherwise (or if no such  $\mathbf{y}$  exists), output 0.

Importantly, the challenger's responses to the verification queries in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  depend  $\mathit{only}$  on the public components (i.e.,  $\mathsf{crs}$  and  $\mathsf{pk}$ ).

For an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we write  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i(\mathcal{A})$  to denote the output distribution of an execution of experiment  $\mathsf{Hyb}_i$  with adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We now show that the output distribution of each adjacent pair of hybrid experiments is statistically indistinguishable.

**Lemma D.2.** For all adversaries A,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0(A) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_1(A)$ .

*Proof.* The statistical distance between  $\mathsf{Uniform}(\mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor})$  and  $\mathsf{Uniform}(\mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'})$  satisfies

<span id="page-56-0"></span>
$$\Delta(\mathsf{Uniform}(\mathbb{Z}_{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor}),\mathsf{Uniform}(\mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'})) = 1 - \frac{2p'q'}{\lfloor N/2 \rfloor} = 1 - \frac{2p'q'}{2p'q'+p'+q'} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \tag{D.1}$$

since  $1/p', 1/q' = \text{negl}(\lambda)$ . Since  $\rho, T = \text{poly}(\lambda)$ , the claim follows by a union bound.

**Lemma D.3.** If  $\mathcal{H}$  is pairwise independent, then for all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1(\mathcal{A}) \overset{s}{\approx} \mathsf{Hyb}_2(\mathcal{A})$ .

*Proof.* In  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , the challenger evaluates  $\mathsf{Verify}$  at most Q+1 times where  $Q=\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  is the bound on the number of queries the adversary makes. For  $j\in\{0,\ldots,Q+1\}$ , let  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j}$  denote the experiment where the first j queries are handling according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$  while the remaining queries are handling according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$ . By construction,  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1 \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_{1,0}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2 \equiv \mathsf{Hyb}_{1,Q+1}$ . Consider  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j}$  for  $j\in[Q+1]$ . These two

experiments only differ in how the challenger computes the output for the  $j^{\text{th}}$  Verify call. Moreover, by construction of  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j}$ , all of the adversary's queries prior to the  $j^{\text{th}}$  query are handled according to the specification in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , which depend only on the public components  $\mathsf{crs}$  and  $\mathsf{pk}$ . Let  $(\sigma, i, r_i, \pi_i)$  be the arguments to the  $j^{\text{th}}$  Verify call. Write  $\sigma = g^c h^{\hat{c}}$  and  $\pi_i = (g^{t_i} h^{\hat{t}_i}, u_i)$  for  $c, t_i \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}$ ,  $\hat{c}, \hat{t}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_2$ , and  $u_i \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ . We consider each case individually:

- Suppose  $r_i \neq \mathsf{LEQ}(g^{t_i}h^{\hat{t}_i}, g^{t_i}h^{\hat{t}_i+1})$ . Then, the output in both experiments is 0.
- Suppose  $\hat{c} \neq 0$  or  $\hat{t}_i \neq 0$ . We argue that with overwhelming probability, the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  is 0. For the output to be 1 in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$ , it must be the case that  $u_i = H(\omega_i)$  where

<span id="page-57-0"></span>
$$\boldsymbol{\omega}_{i} = \left( (g^{t_{i}} h^{\hat{t}_{i}})^{a_{1}} (g^{c} h^{\hat{c}})^{b_{1,i}}, \dots, (g^{t_{i}} h^{\hat{t}_{i}})^{a_{T}} (g^{c} h^{\hat{c}})^{b_{T,i}} \right) \in \mathbb{J}_{N}^{T}. \tag{D.2}$$

In both  $\mathsf{Hyb}_1$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_2$ , the public parameters are independent of the values of  $a_\tau \bmod 2$  and  $b_{\tau,i} \bmod 2$  for all  $\tau \in [T]$ . This follows from the fact that  $a_\tau, b_{\tau,i} \in \mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'}$ , and the adversary only sees elements  $g^{(a_\tau s_i + b_{\tau,i})\mathbf{v}}$ , where g generates a group of order p'q' (and  $\gcd(p'q', 2) = 1$ ). Next, since  $a_\tau$  and  $b_{\tau,i}$  are uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'}$ , the values  $a_\tau \bmod 2$  and  $b_{\tau,i} \bmod 2$  are distributed uniformly and independently of the rest of the public parameters. Since the responses to all of the adversary's queries prior to its  $j^{\text{th}}$  query only depend on the public parameters, the conditional distribution of  $a_\tau \bmod 2$  and  $b_{\tau,i} \bmod 2$  given the adversary's view up to the time of its  $j^{\text{th}}$  query is uniform and independently random. Since at least one of  $\hat{c}$  and  $\hat{t}_i$  is non-zero, this means that the value of  $a_\tau \hat{t}_i + b_{\tau,i} \hat{c}$  mod 2 is independently and uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_2$ , and correspondingly, the conditional min-entropy of the vector  $\omega_i$  from Eq. (D.2) is at least  $T = 2(\lambda + \lceil \log N \rceil)$ . We now appeal to Lemma 2.6 to argue that the output distribution of  $H(\omega_i)$  is  $(3p'q'\varepsilon)$ -close to uniform over  $\{0,1\}^\lambda$  where

$$\varepsilon = 2^{-(T-\lambda)/2} = 2^{-\lambda/2 - \lceil \log N \rceil},$$

since the adversary can choose the values of  $c, t_i \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}$  and  $(\hat{c}, \hat{t}_i) \in \mathbb{Z}_2^2 \setminus \{\mathbf{0}\}$  after seeing the hash function H. Since 3p'q' < N,  $3p'q'\varepsilon < 2^{-\lambda/2} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , so we conclude that conditioned on the adversary's view, the distribution of  $H(\omega_i)$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  is statistically close to uniform over  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . The probability that  $u_i = H(\omega_i)$  is then negligibly close to  $1/2^{\lambda}$ , and the challenger outputs 0 with overwhelming probability.

• Suppose that  $t_i \neq s_i c \in \mathbb{Z}_{p'q'}$ . We only need to consider the case where  $\hat{c} = 0$  and  $\hat{t}_i = 0$ . We show that in this case, the output in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  is 0 with overwhelming probability. For the output to be 1 in  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{1,j-1}$  in this case, we require that  $u_i = H(\omega_i)$  where

<span id="page-57-1"></span>
$$\boldsymbol{\omega}_i = \left(g^{a_1 t_i + b_{1,i} c}, \dots, g^{a_T t_i + b_{T,i} c}\right) \in \mathbb{QR}_N^T. \tag{D.3}$$

The only components in crs and pk that depend on  $a_{\tau}$  and  $b_{\tau,i}$  for  $\tau \in [T]$  are the public-key components  $g^{a_{\tau}s_{i}\mathbf{v}+b_{\tau,i}\mathbf{v}}$ . Let  $\mathbf{b}_{\tau} \in \mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'}^{\rho}$  be the vector whose components are  $b_{\tau,1},\ldots,b_{\tau,\rho}$ , and let  $\mathbf{s} \in \mathbb{Z}_{2p'q'}^{\rho}$  be the vector whose components are  $s_{1},\ldots,s_{\rho}$ . The public parameters  $\mathsf{pk}$  can then be expressed as a function of
